CLEVELAND Sydney
LITERARY NOTES.
A Book of Lovely Lubras.
The latest compliment reaches this locality
with rather a left-handed swing :
. . . The Sydney Bulletin holds the prouc
sway over all Australian print-productions. I
not only reaches the combos and stockmen o
Central Australia, but it reaches lepers on isolatec
islands, lighthouse-keepers that are difficult to
approach, and wild aboriginals have used its rec
cover as the salient point of their costume.
The unapproachable lighthouse-keeper may
pass ; but is it exactly as a leader of leper
opinion, a costumier of wild aboriginals, tha
one would wish to pose ?
* * * *
Mr. Willshire means well, of course. He
is one of the men who know and can’t write
and his booklet, ‘ ‘ The Land of the Dawn
ing ” (W. K. Thomas, Adelaide), collects
under a hackneyed title an aggravating
miscellany of personal narrative, crude
humor, and valuable observations regarding
Northern Territory aborigines—among whom
Mr. Willshire has been policeman, mission
ary, civiliser, king and god for many years
His domain covered the country neighboring
the Katharine, Daly, Gregory, Roper, anc
Victoria rivers; his subjects were cannibals o:
the Australian stone age ; his power was
all but absolute. His duty took him into
many regions untrodden by a white man
among many tribes who had never dreamt o
a white man’s existence ; he saw sights beau
tiful, awful, gorgeous, grotesque, curious
amazing, incredible ; lie mixed familiarly
with strange men, and learnt many strange
things ; and all that he brings back from his
perilous Voyages in the wilderness is a col-
lection of half-articulated tags, and scraps,
and vague hints, of the knowledge seething
within him. He has no voice. Really, it
would pay a literary syndicate to float “ The
Willshire Exploitation Company,” mine for
his ore, and smelt him into jo den speech.
* * * *
The most noticeable thing in Mr. Will-
shire’s book is his warm appreciation of oiu
charming country women of Way-Up. He
positively teems with lovely lubms :
A little blue in off in tin' full bloom of e;irly
loveliness, witli a stalk of red lilies in her banc
(p. 17).
Oro-ginnia Was eighteen, the image of health
and beauty (p. 27).
Poor girl, after marrying Mopie-mopie
she ran away with somebody else, and hit
Mopie-mopie with a yam stick when he came
to recover her. Her end was falling down a
cataract —per Mopie-mopie.
One, who was about eighteen [“ l’&ge critique”
—pour M. Willshire?] the picture of black
beauty and health, was called Billa-bil-100, which
means the king parrot. The other sister, whose
age was about sixteen, was an unblushing bronze,
and naturally timid. She was called Warren-no-
allv, or in English “The maid of the gully.” She
was of good phy-iquo and remarkable beauty
(p. 33;.
■But from between the rocks came a strap-
ping young girl, with the agility of a moun-
tain creature. She jumped from rock to rock,
straight to the grey horse I was silting upon
[natural magnetism, no doubt I], took hold of my
stirrup-iron, and ran alongside until we were out
of danger. She was arrayed in her native modesty,
and I may state this was the prettiest black girl I
ever saw (p. 41).
We ascertained from her that her name was
Puu-garra, which, in the native language, means
a kiss. . . Every line in the contour of her
person was the perfection of feminine beauty.
She moved with a grace beyond all power of re-
production. She had the bust of a Juno, and
hands that would delight a sculptor (p. 42).
The first thing we observed was a beautiful
■ savage maiden, who in her startled movements
was as graceful as a stag (p. 47).
She was a superlatively beautiful girl, with
abnormally beautiful proportions (p. 57).
Out from their flower-festooned alcoves came
the brilliant bevy of blushing maidens, all
timorous and coy (p. 61).
Hundreds of black virgins all nude and in the
full tide of youthful enjoyment, but like chartered
libertines in their conversation, and with languor
like harem denizens (p. 71).
&c., &c., &.— ad. lib.
Let us go to the Northern Territory !
* * * *
And all this time we have been taught by
Brunton Stephens and other poetic truth-
perverters, and by association with the de-
graded tribes of the coast, that the aboriginal
lady was a shapeless creature with a splay
mouth and a soul that never rose above
’bacca ! Let us go to the Northern Terri-
tory, where Mr. Willshire guarantees “to
track and round up now and again,” for the
edification of peripatetic literary persons, ‘ 4 a
bevy of smockless black girls, as sweet in
heart as the morning dew.”
* * * *
There is a legend that the white leader of
black trackers who capture lovely booty is
accorded a certain right which the good old
feudal seigniors claimed at the marriages of
their vassals—but Mr. Willshire says nothing
of this. His attitude is always that of a man
—and a brother. He even recites (on p. 30)
the story of his temptation and triumph when
“breezy gins ” danced round him a seductive
nautch which “about reached its apogee
when each of them had to touch my nose
with her big toe.” Willshire sat quiet and
blushed ; and when one old nigger muttered
something like “ladies’ choice,” and a
“ young woman with a mass of greasy and
tangled curls came and made overtures to
me, whilst the others began romping away up
and down the corroboree ground, I declined
with thanks, and bowed most courteously;
she, with equal politeness, returned the
courtesy.” Mem.—Pungarra, “the Kiss,”
didn’t have greasy and tangled curls.
* * * *
Mr. Wiltshire's conclusion of the whole
matter is noticeable :
Men would not remain so many years in a
country like this if there were no women, and
.... the Almighty .... has placed
them wherever the pioneers go.
What a potentiality of utile cum, dulce lurks in
the human intellect’s neat teleological twist t
* » * *
Away from the lubras Mr. Willshire gives
information only less interesting. He con-
demns black men, white missionaries, and
miscellaneous half-castes. He exalts black
women, some white men, and N.T. scenery.
He gives aboriginal vocabularies, drawings,
and one mysterious inscription. And his
pages are full of ethnological interest, often
excited, but rarely satisfied even as fully as
in this note on “tracking ” :
My native troopers are splendid trackers—never
fail over stones, through spinifex, over high ranges,
through gorges and long grass. If they lose the
tracks for a few minutes their instinct tells-.them
the direction, and on they go. If the travelling is
anything like good, they will jog along on horse
or camel at five miles per hour, appearing not to
be looking down at the tracks ; but still they have
them all the while. A bit of wild f rui t picked off a
tree, a grub taken from the bark of a tree, a
branch broken, a stone turned over, a little
bit of ash from a firestick, a dog’s track, all is
noticed, all is seen, and still we jog along four or
five miles an hour. If the offenders, who know
we are on their tracks, turn and walk backwards,
as they have done in my time, we know it is all
“monkey gammon,’’ they cannot fool the trackers.
Charlotte Waters and the Finke River produce
the most expert trackers the world ever saw. They
begin tracking lizards and snakes, &c., &c., from
their infancy and for their daily sustenance ;
without the wonderful faculty they would
go very hungry. I have noticed little
children who have been asleep in the
wurley, their mothers in the meantime having
walked down to the creek to have a bathe. The
child wakes up and finds its mother gone; it
starts to cry, and all the time walking round the
camp trying to pick up its mother’s track. At
last the child discovers it amongst many others,
and off it runs on the track right to its mother,
who may be half a mile away.
* * * *
It does not need the appendix of testi-
monials to show that Mr. Willshire is a fine,
manly fellow and a capable police officer.
Items.
The chief merit of Louis Becke’s style is its
simplicity ; when he starts to embroider it he
generally loses himself. Here, for example,
is the word -picture which commences “ Lup-
tpn’s Guest ” in his new book (take a long
breath 1) :
A long sweeping curve of coast, fringed
with tall plumed palms casting waving shadows
on the yellow sand as they sway and swish
softly to the breath of the brave trade-wind
that whistles through the thickly - verdured
hummocks on the weather side of the island,
to die away into a soft breath as, after pass-
ing through the belt of cocoanuts, it faintly
ripples the transparent depths of the lagoon—a
broad sheet of blue and silver stretching away
from the far distant western line of reef to the
smooth, yellow beach at the foot of the palms on
the easternmost islet.
Ah-h-h 1 It is lucky this concatenation o
pronouns is unique.
* • • *
In a clever pap. r m the New Review, ex-South
Sea Prime Minister Basil Thomson tells the
curious story of the balolo The balolo is a worm
—living vermicelli, as it were—with no head but
a mouth, and no body but a transparent pipe,
who lives his life deep in the fissures of the coral
reefs, rising twice a year to the upper waters only
to die phoenix-like in the propagation of his kind.
But —here is the curiosity—the balolo is a natural
almanac ; he has a fixed day for his appearance,
and he will not turn therefrom for all the hurri-
canes that ever raged south of the Line. In the
mere observance of seasons and fixed intervals
there would be no greater miracle than our own
bodies can show. The wonder lies in the fact
that the balolo keeps both lunar and solar time,
reconcilingand'adjusting them at regular intervals.
The balolo swarms to the surface on two nights in
the year : the nights of the third quarter of the
moon in October and in November ; and has never
departed from the time during the forty-eight
years it has been watched by Europeans. The
moon directs its choice of the day, the sun its
choice of the month ; and it follows that the
creature cannot maintain regular intervals of
either twelve or thirteen lunations without chang-
ing the calendar month of its re-appearance. For
two years it rises after a lapse of twelve lunations ;
every third year at the thirteenth. But, since
even this arrangemeet will gradually sunder solar
and lunar time, it further intercalates a lunation
once in the twenty-eight years. What are the
immediate impulses, of tide or of season, that
move it to rise on its appointed day, and keep it
back every three and every twenty-eight years,
no theorist has yet essayed to show.
One of the rarest of Australian books is “ The
Life of Emily Duncan ; an Autobiography, with
an Introduction by Robert Coles.” Sydney,
N.S.W., 1878. This is one of the few “objection-
able” productions of the Australian press.
The earliest Australian reprint of an English
book seems to have been “The Posthumous
Papers of the Pickwick Club. With Illustrations
after Phiz. Launceston, Y.D.L., 1838.” The
publisher in his preface says : —“lt is believed
that this is the largest publication which has been
issued from the N.S.W. or the Tasmanian press.
. . . The illustrations will be found fully
equal to the original, and, as the work of a
colonial artist, will preserve to the reprint its
character of a purely colonial publication.”
Christina Rossetti’s posthumous book of poems
is a very flat collection. Every wise author’s
corpse would carry its unpublished MS. along
with it to the crematorium. There is nothing so
aggravating as the zeal of friendly editors to
include “absolutely all the pieces hitherto uncol-
lected ” —because the author knew they weren’t
worth collecting.
Conan Doyle has definitely fixed his level as
that of a storyteller who can while away an hour
or two agreeably, but from whom nothing beyond
the ephemeral must ever be expected. “The
Exploits of Brigadier Gerard ” read well in the
magazines, and they don’t read badly strung to-
gether in a book. Doyle has learnt something
from Weyman, one sees; but he can’t quite mimic
Weyirian’s spontaneity. And the insistence on
English and French national traits becomes mere
caricature.
J. J. M‘Cue’s “Australian Poultry Guide’’
(Geo. Robertson) is a good little manual, with
valuable illustrations.
The best service one can do Anarchist Andrews
Reason is to point out an error in its logic. As
not unusual with logical errors, the fallacy comes
from an intrusive ego : it is comparatively easy to
find flaws in the argument of the other fellow.
Says Reason :
We cannot pay for food, rent, paper, ink, postage, &c.,
•ut of the sum of 30s. per issue, which was about the
return upon our first number from the sales. We have
not received the support vve had every right to expect.
What right has Reason to expect support? Why
is the popular cash to be tethered to its ideals .
Reason has a right to the support it gets ; no
more and no less. When it rises up to declare
that somebody should support it who doesn’t
want to support it, it attempts to infringe on
somebody’s rights in its own interest —which is
bad sense and bad anarchy.
The M.L. Triad prints some ingenious inter-
pretations by local newspapers of the cablegram
that ticketh in darkness. The other day it was
cabled that “doctors are using Rontgen’s dis-
covery with astounding results. Gallstones,
stones in the bladder, and injuries to the bones
are easily seen.” The Wairarapa Daily Times
printed it: —
Doctors are using Rontgen’s discovery with astounding
results. Mr. Gladstone’s stones in the bladder and
injuries to the bones are easily seen.
And at the beginning of the year came a mys-
terious cable about Italy and Abyssinia. The
Wellington Post had it: —
Rome, Jan. Ist.
The Italians have kidnapped two princes who are
closely related to Menelek, ruler of Abyssinia, and who
were travelling in Italy for their education.
The Nelson Mail interpreted it .
Rome, Jan. Ist.
Italian agents in Abyssinia are reported to have success-
fully kidnapped King Menelek, the ruler of the country.
The Nelson Colonist put in it this way :
Rome, Jan. Ist.
Mr. Menlick, who is travelling Italy for eduo.tional
purposes, has disappeared, and it is reported that an
Italian agent has kidnapped him.
No apologies to Victor Daley are needed for
this extract from Tom Bracken’s Annual, «
pleasant M.L. visitor ;
Oh, the Ould Wife, the Ould Wife,
I see her clane, white shelf,
Her dhresser nate, and quite eomplaibe
With chaney and with delf;
Her spinnin’ wheel, her rock an’ reel,
Oh, murther, wirrasthru !
That she, our pride, must stand aside
To give place to the New.
Oh, the New Wife, the New Wife,
Begog, she don’t suit me,
She spinds her time in readin’ up
Works on philosophee;
She niver cooks a mutton chop,
Or makes an Irish stew,
She takes a laid in polvtics—
Toe devil take the new !

BEFORE. YOU
T. E A F ;
When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and C,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
(xeorge-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned ; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “There be land-sharks and water-
sharks,” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round—
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to
Buy Everything

THE STATION BRAND.
< ?/ y° u ln the boots and the long-necked spurs,
x ou ve a nice little hackney there,
rather fancy that brand of hers—
Now what will you take for the mare ?
xou need not go off on too wide a tack,
, t m hardly in want of a horse,
t m only pricing your chestnut hack
tor the sake of the brand, of course.
No thanks ; I don’t fancy exchanges,
Besides, she’s a bit of a screw
As old as the Barrier Ranges,
And shook in the shoulders, too !
Now, what is the use of denial?
Much better have let things stand—•
No, thank you, I want no trial:
I’m buying the Tringa brand !
I know that she’ll carry me fast and far
In waterless waste or wet,
For never the “ TRI and a Bar ”
Was burnt on a bad one yet.
Do I know the brand ? Yes, I think Ido ;
I’ve carried it, hell-fire hot,
To the stockyard fence and passed it through
For many a cleanskin lot;
I’ve heard it hiss on the burning hide
And the short, sharp whinny of pain
As they lifted it off to thrust aside
Or lay to the lines again.
Do I know the brand ? T have watched it streak
To the front in the mustering days—
But why do I tell you—you’ve heard it speak,
And you know what the old hand says !
Enquire of the drovers from North of Bourke,
The Kings of the Overland,
Which are the horses to stand the work :
They will tell you, “ The Tringa Brand ! ”
And question the mailmen in flood-stress met,
Flogging, down in the mud,
Which are the pearls when the plains are wet,
They will tell you, The Tringa Blood ! ”
And ask of the men of the Furthest Back
What their favorite campers are
In the whirling dust when the stockwhips crack ;
And it’s “ TRI and a Bar.”
You can have your price; it’s a lot too much
As horses are selling to-day ;
But a man is a fool and acts as such
When sentiment shows the way ;
But spavined and aged and shoulder-shook,
I'm not regretting the deal,
For the old brand shows like an open book
What nothing else can reveal—
The far-off life with its witching charms
And the glamour of sun and star
In the happy days when our coat-of-arms
Was “ TRI and a Bar.”
GLENROWAN.
THE ANSWERING STRIDE.
I bred him and trained him and rode him,
He had plenty of heart and could stay ;
But the pace of the others soon showed him
He would want all his talent that day.
I feared they would give him the slip—
A young one who’d scarcely been tried,
But I gave him a clip with the spurs and the whip
And he won with his answering stride.
A soft one with breed and condition
And a bit of a pull in the weight
May smother a weak opposition
And win by the length of the straight.
But when you’re on one that won’t flinch,
A bundle of heart in a hide,
Nothing gives you each inch when it comes to the
pinch
Like the spur and the answering stride.
Your whip may be pride or ambition,
Perhaps a craving for money your spur;
Love of kudos, or social position,
Or perhaps it may only be “ her.”
But if you can carry the weight,
And you don t know you cannot till tried,
Take each lick of hard fate and you’ll win soon or
late
If you’ve grit for the answering stride.
Maoriland.
WAIHOA.
“ Canard ” arose from a French journalist
telling a yarn about his 20 ducks. He killed one,
and the other 19 ate it. So, till only two ducks
remained, and one ate the other.
A Sydney reporter had his pocket picked
the other day—by two newsboys who were trying
to sell him his own paper ! The poor scribe lost a
broken knife, some notes on a picnic, the result of
Tatt’s sweep, and an invite to a gospel meeting.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

tion — i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, APRIL 25, 1896.
To the Protectionists of N.S. Wales.
The recent election at Waverley (N.S.W.)
showed that in a great Foreign-trade strong-
hold the importers’ majority has largely
declined, and this fact has put a certai
amount of confidence into the Protections
party. The Cowra election, which followec
shortly afterwards, showed that in a Prc
tectionist stronghold the previously sma
minority of the shoddy party had grown sti
smaller, and this further encouraged th
N.S.W. National Party to look forward t
the good time coming when they will anne
the portfolios and the salaries. In th
ordinary course of events nothing can pre
vent its turn coming by-and-bye, unles
the party invents some even more effectual
method of cutting its own throat than ha
yet been discovered. The arrival of genuin
Freetrade in everything save alcohol ant
tobacco, which is due about the year 1900 o
so, if nothing occurs in the interim, will do
more to damage the importers’ cause than
anything that has yet happened in the
province. The fact that the old, conserva
tive Calico Jimmy element and the new
Labor element can never really coalesce is
another factor which tends to break up the
Foreign-trade party. The other fact that the
community grows weary of every Ministry,
sooner or later, and that Government by
Contradiction consists mostly in putting out
the man that the nation has grown tired of,
and replacing him by the man that it was
tired of previously, makes it dead certain
that the Protectionists will get their oppor-
tunity sooner or later. How much later it
will be, and how long the opportunity will
last when it comes, and when it goes how
long it will be before it comes again, are
matters which depend almost wholly on the
party itself and on its leaders. The Bulle-
tin therefore invites the Protectionist body
to seriously consider the position of its re-
presentatives in Parliament, and how they
got into that position, and to compare it with
the one they occupied a very few years ago,
and to weep over the result of the investi-
gation.
* * * *
A few years back the Protectionists were
the democratic element of N.S.W. Assembly.
They were the supporters of the land and
income tax policy, the men who wanted to
explode the great land monopoly, the main
supporters of payment of members and one-
man-one-vote. On more than one occasion
they pushed direct taxation measures through
the Assembly in the face of bitter opposition
from the Free-raid and Calico Jimmy inter-
est, only to lose them in a hostile Upper
House, which was solid almost to the last
fossil on Cobdenism. In those days the
State Land Bank, the State Bank of Issue,
the Referendum, the Abolition of Party Go-
vernment, the Woman Franchise, and other
burning questions of to-day had hardly begun
to arrive in politics; but, if they had ar-
rived, there is hardly a doubt, from the atti-
tude of the two parties, that the Protection-
ists would have supported them heartily, and
that the Foreign-trade-revenue-tariff men
would have been furiously hostile. Every
democrat worth calling such among the poli-
ticians of a few years ago was in the ranks of
the Protectionists ; the party was honored with
the loathing of the Legislative Council—the
surest proof that it deserved the confidence
of the people ; and the S.M. Herald looked
upon it with disgust, whereas now that
paper regards it rather as an erring brother.
And on the other side of the House were
the old Freetraders, the softgoods element
in the frock-coat and the shiny pot-hat, the
big landlords, the property-suffrage and vote-
for-thrift men, the solid individuals who
hated the very name of direct-taxation, the
worst class of religious bigots, the great mass
of the Upper House, Abigail, Parkes,
John Davies, the Yellow Pup generally,
and everything that was effete and moss-grown
and crusted over with the ancient crust of
time and prejudice.
* * * *
Then the unstable and incompetent leaders
of the National cause fooled their great chance
away. The game of Government by Con-
tradiction is at all times a difficult one to
play, and Dibbs played it badly, while Lyne
played it still worse. The Freetraders took
up the Land-and-Income tax, which they
had formerly opposed with all their power
and influence, and the Protectionist leaders,
who had formerly fought in defence of that
principle, drifted into opposing it simply
because it is the habit of the Opposition,
under the beautiful party system, to oppose
everything. The Freetraders, who had
urged the Upper House by frantic peti-
tions to reverse the will of the people’s
representatives by rejecting the Dibbs tariff,
attempted to curtail the power of that
aggressive body in order to prevent it doing
the very thing which they had previously, in
another set of conditions, urged it to do ;
and the Protectionist leaders, instead of
assisting them in the work, wandered by
their feeble policy of drift into being the
champions of their old enemy. Thereupon
the Upper House extended to them the right
hand of friendship, and with a lamentable
want of foresight they accepted the clammy
and shapeless offering and appeared before
the country as the champions and accom-
plices of the Fat Man chamber. They
attempted, through the mouth of G. Repub-
lican Dibbs, to repudiate the principle of
Payment of Members, of which they had
been the warmest advocates—apparently
because its former opponents, G. Reid and
Co., had become favorable to it. The Pro-
tectionist party, after a long and hard battle,
abolished the property vote in New South
Wales, and its tactless Parliamentary bosses
even began to show hostility to their own
great work when their enemies commenced
to approve of it. The Australian Star, the
recognised organ of the party, by its gradual
drift from Liberalism to advanced Toryism,
showed how the Protectionist leaders fell
from grace, just as the Telegraph, the
Foreign-trade organ, by its drift from
Liberalism to an even worse variety of Tory-
ism, shows how the bosses of the Calico
Jimmy interest would gladly fall also, if the
pressure of the Labor-party did not keep
them progressing. And the Protectionist
bosses fell simply because Lyne and Dibbs
stuck to the old party theory that an Oppo-
sition should oppose the Government in all
things, right or wrong ; that it should be
against the Government even if it was doing
the things which the Opposition had consis-
tently advocated ; and that the beginning,
middle, and end of its existence was to be
against the Government.
* * * *
There was no reason, apart from the desire
to be against the Government, why they
should have drifted into this fatal position.
In Maoriland the Protectionist party is also
the Labor party and the party which upholds
the land and income taxes, the State Land
Bank, woman suffrage, the compulsory eight-
hours’ system, the minimum wage, the State
insurance system, and the most advanced
political programme ever put forward by any
Australasian Government. In Victoria it
is the party which supports manhood and
womanhood suffrage, land and income tax,
State banking, the suppression of sweating
and bogus banks, the reform of the Legisla-
tive Council, and every other democratic
movement. In South Australia it is the
democratic party pure and simple. In N.S.
Wales it was the democratic party, but its
leaders, in their utter inability to see beyond
the miserable party exigencies of the moment,
have insisted on identifying it with every
Tory and every Tory movement which pro-
mised temporary assistance against the
Government. By thus selling their
democratic principles to buy the feeble
support for the moment of forty or fifty
ancient incapables in the Upper House, they
threw away the support later on of 40,000 or
50,000 democratic And they did not
even get the price of their ruinous bargain,
for the M’s.L.O. keep dying off, and the Go-
vernment replaces them with new men who
are younger and not so fat, and who know
not Lyne, so that the vote of the democratic
section of the community was mostly lost,
and that of the Upper House was not per-
manently gained.
• * * *
Idle situation now is a noble example of
the workings of Government by Contradic-
tion. Since the Dibbs-Lyne Ministry estab-
lished manhood suffrage pure and simple,
the vote of N.S. Wales is mainly a democratic
vote. The Protectionist leaders waited long
for the chance of appealing to this demo-
cratic vote, and apostatised just when it
came. On the fiscal question the country is
probably about equally divided. The Pro-
tectionist party represents the democracy
quite as well as the other, if not better.
Protection is the policy of the workman who
wants work at making boots and all manner
of other things for the local market, and the
farmer who wants a local market for his
butter and wheat and fruit. Freetrade is the
policy of the big importer in York-street,
Sydney, and his clients and dependents,
and of the squatter, whose wool goes abroad
in any case, and who views a good local
market for produce merely as a thing which
makes the pestilent selector multiply on his
run. Therefore, the Protectionist party is
at least as democratic as the other. It has
quite as much reason as the other to desire
the bursting up of big estates by a Land-tax
or otherwise ; the establishment of a State
Land Bank to suppress usurious rates of in-
terest ; the creation of a State Bank of Issue
which won’t burst and thereby create panics
and dislocate industry ; the minimum wage ;
the reform of the Council, which mainly re-
presents its inveterate enemies, the big
land-grabber and the usurer; and every other
good thing which the Reid Ministry has
offered or promised. And, in addition to
these, it wants Protection, for the sufficient
reason that it wants work and wages above
all other things.
* » * *
Meanwhile, circumstances over whicli the
Protectionists of the country at large have
no control, have made Lyme their principal
representative in Parliament. He was
appointed because he has great ability as
leader of an Opposition in the party sense—
that is to say, he has great ability in ex-
posing the errors of his opponents, and
stumps the country eloquently and inde-
fatigably in the party’s interests, and can
manage elections admirably, and will some
day, without doubt, if he lives and retains
his position, lead his followers back to
power—and won’t have the faintest idea what
to do with it, in any large and statesmanlike
sense, when he gets there. The party can’t
get rid of its able and brilliant incubus,
except by throwing him out at next election,
and that, in all probability, can only be done
by putting in a Freetrader and endorsing
Freetrade ; and the party in general can’t
do it—only the small section which consti-
tutes the Hume electorate ; and even if it
was done some other electorate would pro-
bably return the ejected one at the first
opportunity. The party in general can’t ex-
plain in any effectual way that it wants Pro-
tection and doesn’t want Lyne, or that
it wants Protection and Lvne, but that
it respectfully requests Lyne to add a
number of other democratic reforms to his
programme in addition to Protection, and if
it doesn’t want, Lyne it can’t signify what
other leader it does want. It can’t explain
when it returns Lyne and See by big
majorities, that it does so only because they
support Protection, and not because they
oppose almost every other of the country’s
democratic aspirations. It can’t make it
plain to these gentlemen which of their prin-
ciples it disapproves of, and thus give them
a chance to reform. It can’t explain any-
thing, m fact; it can only put them in, which
means enthusiastic approval of their Conser-
vatism, or put their opponents in, which
means whole-souled approval of their policy
of destroying local industries for the benefit
of the importer. _ The Protectionist leaders
now that they have relapsed into crusted
loryism, offer the country practically no-
thing except Protection; and the country
will accept only when it wants Protection so
very badly indeed that it will let every other
democratic aspiration go for the sake of
getting it.

How the Q. Government Party Won the
Elections.
As anticipated, the Queensland Government
party has been victorious at the general elec-
tion, and returns to power—numerically, at
least—almost as strong as in last Parliament.
Brisbane Courier, and Sydney Morning
Herald, and Melbourne Argus —and doubt-
less all the other Australasian Tory organs—
have duly congratulated it upon this result.
All that is left to the democratic press is to
congratulate it upon the manner in which this
result was accomplished.
* * * *
The Q. Govt, party won the elections by the
aid of the plural or property-vote—the vote
which measures a man not by what he is, but
by what he owns ; the vote which weighs flesh
and blood against bricks and mortar ; the vote
which worships the land-jobber, and the boom-
speculator, and the dummy-swindler, and'snaps
its fingers in the face of struggling industry;
the vote which counts wealth a glory to a man,
and poverty his abiding dishonor ; the vote
which declares that the ideal citizen is not an
Abraham Lincoln, but a Jay Gould, not the
toiler who bears the burdens of the people, but
the financial robber who exploits the people,
and founds the aggrandisement of an individual
upon the sufferings of a community. The
Government majority does not represent Man-
hood ; it represents Mortgages.
* * * *
The Q. Govt, party won the elections by
systematic disfranchisement of the electors
opposed to it. This disfranchisement began
with the absolute refusal to so reform the elec-
toral law that a man, in theory entitled to vote,
might vote wherever he chanced to be within
the province. The chief industries of Queens-
land are wool-growing and mining ; it is upon
these that she depends to balance imports with
exports ; it is from the profits of these that she
pays the interest upon her large foreign debt;
these stand between her and public and private
insolvency. Her agriculture, her manufactures,
are still little more than self-supporting. Here
is no reason to show favor to the agriculturist
and the artisan. But the artisan and the agri-
culturist are naturally confined to the districts
where they labor; they are stay-at-homes;
they have votes. Many miners, many men en-
gaged in the wool industry, are inevitably driven
to travel from place to place in search of profitable
employment; they are rovers ; they have no
votes. The injustice cries for remedy ; but the
bush laborers are notorious opponents of the
Govt, party, and the cry is stifled. The Queens
land Government is a public garrotter.
* * * *
ihe Q. Govt, party won the elections by
shamelessly prostituting the machinery of go-
vernment to partisan ends. The Premier of the
province put himself at the head of a Political
Association whose chief mission was the dis-
franchisement of democratic voters. From the
Premier s office, over the Premier’s signature,
was issued circular after circular on behalf of
this, class organisation, exhorting the “purg-
ing of the rolls, the selection of “good men”
as agents and candidates, the defence of the
privileges of the propertied class, the pre-
vention of the spread of democratic principles.
he electoral registrars and electoral revision
courts continually felt the pressure of this
association—with the Premier’s name and offi-
cial authority behind it—urging, on various
pretexts, that the names of its suspected politi-
cal opponents be struck off the electoral rolls.
he levising magistrates—mostly nominees and
supporters of the Government—needed little
persuasion to deal severely with the voter who
based his claim to the franchise merely on
ns manhood; but, if any persuasion was
needed, the formal union of the head of the
Government with the Political Association sup-
phed it. This prostitution of the Premier’s
office sanctified party dishonesty, as thousands
of voteless Democrats to-day are well aware.
-IN or, even when the Democratic voter had
passed every legal pitfall, complied with every
formal punctilio, and thoroughly proved his
tight to vote, was his vote always secured to
him. At the revision courts, last January,
■ '1 new v °ters were added to the list. These,
tu the ordinary course, would have been entitled
to vote in April, and Parliament does not meet
till June. But the spies of the Political Asso-
ciation considered that most of these new
'oteis were adverse to the Government, so the
elections, which normally should have taken
place at the end of April, were antedated a
month, and the 3577 new voters were thus dis-
franchised. Thanks to its principals, the
V Govt, party can dispense with principles.

PLAIN ENGLISH.
Trustees and Trustees.
Victorian Judges are humorous always when
dealing with the matter of fraudulent trustees.
Justice Hood awhile ago made a painfully
funny remark upon the conduct of an unfor-
tunate legal gentleman who had “applied a
client’s money to his own use, and when asked
for it was not able to comply with the request.”
According to his Honor, the aforesaid failure to
cash-up trust-money on demand “ would be
little or no offence in a layman, but it was an
offence in a professional man.” After which
Justice Hood reprimanded the delinquent, or
suspended him from practice for 48 hours, or
did something equally comic under the circum-
stances. It was not an isolated instance of
judicial waggery where fraudulent trusteeism
is concerned. Other Judges have addressed
other such solicitors in exactly the same mild
spirit, and the fact that these professional mis-
appropriators of coin are privileged to apply it
to their own uses is quite notorious—in Smell-
bourne. Nevertheless, one would expect the
potentates of the Bench to forbear from rubbing
the joke into the community’s callous hide.
Not so. The other day Judge Casey passed
sentence upon a youthful P.O. who had
been convicted of secreting lialf-a dozen letters
in his pocket. Doubtless the lad intended to
stick to the letters, although it happened he
was able to produce them, unopened, when
taxed with the offence. Judge Casey made a
speech to the prisoner. He said that the
larcenous letter-sorter, trembling behind the
spikes, was “ in the position of a fraudulent
trustee , and must be made an example of.” And
his Honor did make an example of the youth,
to the tune of 18 months’ hard labor. The
sentence was not unduly harsh, but the joke
was unintentionally cruel. Had that convicted
post-office understrapper occupied the position
of a solicitor who applies a client’s property to
his own use— i.e., had he been before the court,
though not behind the spikes, in the capacity of
a fraudulent legal trustee—he would have gone
forth with his tongue in his respectable cheek,
and no official stain (worth mentioning) on his
character. As it was, he went weeping away
under the wings of two gaol-warders.
A Tory Philanthropist.
Maoriland Tory party wants the limitations
on child-labor removed —in the interests of the
children. (The Vic. Tory party also wants no
limitations put on sweating, in the interests of
the sweated ln fact, it is always the
interests of the poor employe that the lory
party is thinking about). Button, M.H.R.,
voices the opinions of the party thus :
As to the limitation of child-labor, there were many in-
stances already where work which would be of immense
advantage to many families could not be got by boys and
girls who had not passed a certain standard—(A Voice :
“ A good thing, too.”) Was it altogether a, good thing ?
Was it better to “ cram ” a starving child with book learn-
ing or with bread and butter ? —(Applause.)
This, of course, shows that children of any age
should be allowed to toil in factories and work-
shops. Then, the limitations being removed by
the sympathetic Button kindof man, the Button
variety of employer would get his work done
bv ignorant, toil-worn, joyless, stunted children
at ss. a week instead of paying £2 a week to
adults, and the adults would either have to
work cheaper to compete with the children or
else be unemployed, and the “starving
children ” would increase tenfold in number
through the reduction of wages and the in-
crease of competition. Of course, if the
family Button alluded to could put its children
on the labor-market at the present rate of
wages, which rate is maintained by keeping
other children out cf the labor-market, things
would improve for it. But if all children were
treated similarly, then the wages of the adults
of the family would go down with a thud, and
the starvation would remain the same, and the
only gainer would be the employer, as repre-
sented by Button, who would get labor at
greatly reduced rates. Button, by the way,
doesn’t venture to allege that he knows any
family in the painful condition described; he
merely says, as a reason for abolishing the
limitations upon child-labor, that a hungry m '
fant wants food more than learning. Which is
quite as much to the point as if he said that a
cow wants grass rather than a family Bible, and
proved thereby that the Bible should be abol-
ished and the whole country sown with grass
from one end to the other.
Honorary Fat Men.
Dear Bulletin, -Honorary Fat Man be be
1 P or M L.C., must be dismissed in Victoria
before decent systems of government and justice
prevail. The present unpaid M.L.C. is a Mg
block in the way of reform, because only tuft-
hunters guinea pigs, and patronage-mongers
care for f post for which there is no direct
emolument— and the range of candidates offer
Tncr is so limited that the electors rarely have
any choice worth speaking of. The one tnft-
hunter in the field nets the position by default.
A nominee Upper House or a paid Uppet House
would be better than the present Vic. iambus
but reformers stand aghast at a House that was
designed to be incorrigible and irreclaimable,
and which answers its purpose. The best hope
is deferred hope; tlie Council must be fought
on its own ground, Fat Men a little less offen-
sive than the present crowd must be brought in,
and the House amended by slow degrees. Mean-
while, the present writer wants to make a
suggestion for hurrying up things. It is to
pay members of the Council (as Premier King-
ston has done in South Australia). The
method would be to provide, say, <£2oo per
man, or £9600, in the Appropriation Act,
and thus bring plenty of candidates into
the field at each election. The Council would
resist this item in the Appropriation Act, but
the Assembly could cheerfully wait for its sur-
render. The Council would not dare to throw
out the whole Act, and thus precipitate another
Black Wednesday at this juncture. A Black
Wednesday now would mean five more big
financial institutions and a shoal of small
in liquidation, with the attendant exposures of
“ reputable and respectable ” directors in the
Council and out of it. A Council containing 29
directors, many of them directors of very shaky
institutions, would accept the insult—and the
honorarium. Two objections will be raised to
this suggestion. First, that it is unconstitu-
tional, to which I offer no demur, only stipulat-
ing that is practicable. The other objection is
that it would be rewarding the obstinacy of
those in the Council, and still having a long
lease of membership to run. As a fact it would
cause life-long chagrin to at least 40 (ominous
number) landlords in the Council to have such a
novel and vital influence imported into their
elections, and any little advantage they might
gain for a few sessions would be compensated
by their inevitable defeat at the polls a little
later on—and also by the country’s gain in the
long run.—F.
Because the “Dons” of Sydney University
have shut-down hard upon the senseless repro-
duction here of an asinine English University
custom, the undergraduates got the sulks so
badly that they “ wouldn’t play,” and boycotted
the Commemoration ; but the Commemoration
got along all right, and the sun rose as usual
next morning. The Pundits, in fact, have
come out distinctly on top. The average
Briton’s share of the vis comica doesn’t run to
much beyond the baldest buffoonery. It is not
so much “ sadly ” that he takes his pleasure as
uncouthly, and the dismal tom-fooleries of
English University Commemorations haven’t
even the modest excuse of being funny. They
have immemorial usage, it is contended, behind
them, but the mere antiquity of a custom is, as
often as not, prima-facie evidence of its fatuity.
A tradition of blatant imbecility, at all events,
is a good one not to follow, and the Australian
alumnus, in striving to ape the clumsy rowdyism
of his beef-witted English analogue, is but
doing profitless injustice to his own more agile
mentality and more common-sense environment.
Let him originate for himself a rational
academic legend —not blindly copy what is
either a barbarism or a puerility or both.
Deakin, who spoke at last week’s Melb. anti-
sweating meeting, does not emerge from the
business as well as could have been hoped. One
of his heaviest charges against the Leg. Council
was that it limited the operation of the proposed
Factories Bill to three years. Sargood came out
in next day’s paper with the statement that
Deakin, when lunching with Sargood, origin-
ated the limitation. Deakin’s reply was that
he only referred to the disputed Permit-clause
but common people ask amazedly why Deakin
lunches with his political enemies, and whether
it is a confirmed habit. Furthermore, Deakin
tried to get in a good word for Sargood at the
anti-sweating meeting, but the good word was
howled at, and Deakin saved himself by a
violent peroration which might, and might not,
mean a good deal. Vacillation and compromise
have dogged Deakin too long, and it is due to
himself that he reform. Let him learn a verse
from the Song of Compromise :
I paralyze the hand of God
When He would loose at last
The cataract of vengeance due
For age-old crimes of caste !
Lands - Minister Carruthers, in publicly
threatening that he intended to' scrutinise the
bond-Mes of. every “conditional purchase
taken up under the Land Act of 61, or any
subseouent Act up to that of last year, was
merelv breathing wind. It is certain that all
c.p’s under the ’6l Act have either been forfeited
or certificated—many of them have passed
through several hands —and are now fairly
beyond any Ministerial reach. It is probable
also that dummying was absolutely legal in
New South Wales until ’75, and it is hard to
see how the law can go behind that fact. Again,
the Act of ’B4 provides definitely for the safety
of then existing certificated c.p’s. Yet, because
of the Carruthers threat, the New South
Wales banks are alleged—very much alleged,
apparently—to have begun to shut down on
advances to, and to call up the overdrafts of,
all squatters and others in re c.p. holdings.
And from a business point of view, the banks
are justified in doing so. Not that the “Crown
can absolutely regain the c.p’s, but because of
the possible tremendous cost of law proceedings,
if they should be conducted after the deadly
fashion of the Mercadool cases. Adding this
Hay terror to the Landaninkum tax, there is
new force in the advice—“ go on the land,
young man ; ” and new joy for the man who
has gone there.
The other day “ Lady ” Stout, wife of Robert,
K C M.G., put a dreadful spoke in her hus-
band’s wlieel for next election by expressing, at
a public meeting and in the most distinct terms,
her approval of Chinese immigration and her
admiration of the nice, clean, civil, industrious,
thrifty Chinaman. Stout has been an unsatis-
factory Democrat for a long time past. He has
been trying for a number of years to make
people forget his alliance with Vogel and his
awful Midland Railway. He tried hard to
succeed Ballance in the Premiership, and
if he had done so he was quite will-
ing to be an advanced Liberal and
follow in the dead leader’s footsteps. He didn t
get there, however, so he has been the candid
and disagreeable friend of th ®
don Ministry ever since. He has careiuiiy
avoided showing any signs of Toryism while he
was awake, in public at all events, but pro-
bably he has made wild remarks in his sleep
about his inclination to join Russell, and upset
the whole Government apple-cart, and bring in
Chinamen and land-grant railways, and stoush
Seddon in one act. Probably, also, he has made
similar observations over the mutton at dinner,
when he was unusually grumpy, and his wife,
thinking it was all real, has given Robert away
in public—the private Robert who was never
intended for publication—and created an im-
pression which he won’t get over for another 10
years, at least. No one will believe that Mrs.
Stout wasn’t voicing the hidden sentiments of
Robert, K.C.M.G., even though that lady
protests in sackcloth and ashes and tears, on
the platform of the Town Hall ; and the
Premiership of a Liberal Ministry, which
Stout has been waiting for so patiently,
looks as if it was gone for ever. The moral
is that no man should accept a title, and by
making his wife a “ Lady ” cause her to be
rushed after for platform-oratory purposes,
because every man’s wife knows him as some-
thing quite different from what he wants the
public to think he is, and she sees so much of
the domestic Robert that she is apt to forget
the other one. No public man would last long
if his wife took to explaining him as she sees
him. Supposing the wife of Premier Reid took
to believing and publicly expounding her hus-
band’s private views about the N.S.W. Labor-
party, for instance !
A new terror to newspaper-proprietors, &c.,
and a new source of income to “supers” and
others, are threatened as the result of a late
English libel-case. In Russell v. Notcutt, a
theatrical artiste recovered £lOO damages for a
libel which consisted in publishing her “ out of
her legitimate order on the playbill.” In this
case defendant apparently did it after his at-
tention had been called to it, but there are loud
complaints by him and many English papers
that it was held to be unnecessary that plaintiff
should prove that there was “ express malice ”
in the misplacing. As a matter of fact, the
law implies malice from the fact of publica-
tion. The case is certainly a peculiar one, and
may have consequences serious enough to war-
rant its consideration in any reforms in the
anomalous libel-law. It might possibly con-
trol the case of a newspaper reporting the
lawyers engaged in a case out of the order of
precedence ; and who is to tell that it may not
afford a cause of action to a Catholic archbishop
who is named in a Guvmentouse function after
a Protestant bishop ?
The attention of ladies interested in raising the
“age at which a mere ignorant child is per-
mitted by law to consent to her own infamy ”
is directed to the proceedings at Warragul
(Vic.) police court on April 14, when a “ mere
ignorant child ” only 11£ years old prosecuted
her adopted father on a charge of criminal
assault set forth with the greatest circumstan-
tiality. The monster guilty of this outrage
was not, however, punished, for the simple
reason that there wasn’t any monster and there
wasn’t any outrage, and the “ mere ignorant
child” of 11J went into the witness-box and
swore with the greatest coolness that she had
fabricated the entire story because her adopted
father was cross with her and she “ thought
she would like to go away for a change.” The
natural yearning for a “ change ” of the hys-
terical young female in a monotonous locality,
and the manner in which she secures it—as at
Gunnedah, Warragul, and a dozen other places
recently—at the expense of the most convenient
male, is suggested as a nice subject for the
age-of-consent essayists to spread themselves
over. The Warragul innocent, by the way,
goes scot free, as usual. There doesn’t seem
much else to do with a young lady whose
natural cussedness breaks out so early ; still
the advocates for “ equal laws for women ” may
note that she does go free.
It looks hard to the needy authorities of Sydney
Zoo that the Treasury should refuse the usual
£ for £ grant on the £5O of salary which secre-
tary Catlett has foregone for the benefit of the
Society. But the Treasury principle is the
sound one. Not once or twice hospital officials,
especially in outlying parts of Australia, have,
with the design of bulkily adding to their
funds, fixed up employes on a generous scale,
the understanding being that such employb
would denote £lO, £2O or £3O of the amount to
the respective institutions. The writer recalls
a case in which an hospital secretary, who
would have accepted the post at £5O, was
offered £lOO on the specified condition of giving
back £5O, which, on the Government plan of
£ for £ supplied the grocer-souled committee
with a secretary gratis. Mr. Catlett has been
on a fixed salary for many years, and as his
donation is but recent it is obvious that no
suspicion attaches to the Zoo committee ; but
if the Treasury honored the claim it would
suggest more wrongdoing to various bodies of
smart officials than it perpetrates by repu-
diating the Society’s claim in the present in-
stance.
Attorney-General Byrnes is of opinion that
Queensland can’t afford to federate lest its
trade might go to Sydney instead of to Bris-
bane (The idea that Queensland exists tor
anv purpose except to supply trade to Brisbane
probably doesn’t occur to Byrnes.) It is diffi-
cult to take Nelson’s dreadfully smart young
man seriously in this matter. Under Federa-
tion there will be one railway system, pre-
sumably one uniform railway rate, and no
border tariff; therefore trade must tend to
make for the nearest port, other things being
equal. Brisbane is a nearer port for all South
Queensland than Sydney or Newcastle, and it
should not only keep all the South Queensland
trade, but annex that of New South Wales as
far south as Glen Innes. If it doesn t, it can
only be because Brisbane is such a lamentable
port that people will pay higher railway rates
to ship their produce by Sydney or Newcastle.
But even at the worst, Federation should
help Brisbane, to some extent, at New South
Wales’s expense, by reason of its advantage in
distance, and Byrnes’s story is too thin. Ihe
real trouble is that the Fat Men of the nigger-
haunted land of leprosy don’t want to federate
with the beastly Democrats of New South
Wales, Victoria, South Australia, and possibly
Maoriland, and give all these people a voice in
their affairs. Federation will be fatal to the
Fat Man regime Up North.
A vociferous milkman, one O’Keefe, has been
fined ss. at South Melbourne for crying “ Milk,
O ! ” on a Sunday morning, in the vicinity of a
place of worship. The noisy merchant made
himself heard within the tabernacle, and the
saints couldn’t pray for thinking that he was
a ha’penny a quart cheaper than their own milk-
man. Or else he clashed with the shepherd’s
discourse. Anyway, O’Keefe got fined. The
Loard’s Christian servants appear to have
things all their own way on Sundays. If a
sinner arranges a card-party, or an amateur
concert, he can’t effectively prosecute the
aggressive street-preacher who chooses to give
tongue on the kerbstone outside his place of
worship. The pious nuisance howls with im-
punity, whereas the useful member of society
who retails suction for the baby and liquid
ingredient for the toothsome custard-pudding
is forbidden to proclaim the Word. And this
reminds us that the law doesn’t protect the
Jewish Sabbath from street cries. O’Keefe
might stop his cart at the gates of the Syna-
gogue on the last day of the week and give ’em
“ Milk, 0 ! ” till further orders.
Melbourne Argus contends that the assertion
that a land-tax will reduce the value of land
and so enable the poor man to occupy it “is as
ridiculous as it is immoral ” :
For prices can only be reduced by rendering the land
less profitable than before, and the smaller the profit the
less the land is fit for the poor man’s occupation, and the
more is it likely to fall into the hands of capitalists who
can work it on a large scale.
The one conclusion to be drawn from this gospel
is that the poor man can never get near the
land on any terms. If it is cheap it must be
unprofitable, and will starve him ; if it is pro-
fitable, it is dear, and he can’t pay for it. The
Argus overlooks, or fails to understand, the
fact that a scientific land-tax does not decrease
the producing value of land, but only its specu-
lative value. Land in Collins-street would be
more valuable to the country and quite as
valuable to those who work upon it if it paid a
bouncing value-tax, but it would be of less
value to the landlord. Same with country
land. That vast tract between Melbourne and
Geelong, held by the Cijirnsides, would, under
the stimulus of a stiff graduated land-tax, soon
become of tremendous value to the people and
the State, because it would no longer pay the
Chirnsides to run a few sheep upon miles and
miles of arable land within almost a stone’s
throw of metropolitan markets.
In additions to the arguments often given
against allowing Judges too much rope in
deciding questions of fact, on account of their
hermit life and want of knowledge of the real
world, the following from “Junius ” on lawyers
generally is worth noting :
As to lawyers, their profession is supported by the in-
discriminate defence of right and wrong. ... As a
practical profession, the study of the law requires but a
moderate portion of abilities. The learning of a pleader
is usually upon a level with his integrity. The indis-
criminate defence of right and wrong contracts the under-
standing, while it corrupts the heart. Subtlety is soon
mistaken for wisdom, and impunity for virtue. If there
be any instances upon record, as some there are un-
doubtedly, of genius and morality united in a lawyer,
they are distinguished by their singularity, and operate
as exceptions.
The extract has especial point just now when
there appears to be a tendency to the indis-
criminate appointment of stipendiary magis-
trates from the ranks of the lawyers alone.
Hay, and Hell, and Booligal,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“You come and see me, boys,” he said ;
“ You’ll find a welcome and a bed
And whisky any time you call,
Although our township hasn’t got
The name of quite a lively spot —
You see, I live in Booligal.
“ And people have an awful down
Upon the district and the town—
Which worse than hell itself they call;
In fact, the saying far and wide
Along the Riverina side
Is ‘ Hay and Hell and Booligal.’
“ No doubt it suits ’em vfery well
To say it’s worse than Hay or Hell,
But don’t you heed their talk at all.
Of course, there’s heat—no one denies—
And sand and dust and stacks of flies,
And rabbits, too, at Booligal.
“ But such a pleasant, quiet place,
You never see a stranger’s face—
They hardly ever care to call.
The drovers mostly pass it by ;
They reckon that they’d rather die
Than spend a night in Booligal.
“ The big mosquitoes frighten some—
You’ll lie awake to hear ’em hum—
And snakes about the township crawl;
But shearers, when they get their cheque,
They never come along and wreck
The blessed town of Booligal.
“ But down in Hay the shearers come
And fill themselves with fighting-rum,
And chase blue devils up the wall,
And fight the snaggers every day,
Until there is the deuce to pay —
There’s none of that in Booligal.
“ Of course, there isn’t much to see—
The billiard-table used to be
The great attraction for us all,
Until some careless, drunken curs
Got sleeping on it in their spurs,
And ruined it, in Booligal.
“ Just now there is a howling drought
That pretty near has starved us out—
It never seems to rain at all;
But, if there should come any rain,
You couldn’t cross the black-soil plain
You’d have to stop in Booligal.”
“ We’d have to stop ! ” With bated breath
We prayed that both in life and death
Our fate in other lines might fall :
“ Oh, send us to our just reward
In Hay or Hell, but, gracious Lord,
Deliver us from Booligal'. ”

Hie step from Oscar Wilde’s fluffy comedy
to the tragic “ Fedora ” is a long and sudden one,
but the B. and B. co. get over it without acci-
dent. “Fedora” opened well, and, if the public
appreciation of Saturday night rests on a sub-
stantial cash basis, it ought to be a paying spec.
It has all the thrill of melodrama, without the
corpse being dropped with too conspicuous a slump
right behind the footlights, and as the time usually
employed in making the corpse, finding, diagnos-
ing, and removing it, wiping up the blood-stain
and similar menial offices, is all saved, there is so
much the more leisure for thrill. The crime itself
is a gentlemanly, evening-dress affair which no one
can object to. But “Fedora” is a work of art
mainly as an essay on the cussedness of things in
general, and their unspeakable tendency to go
askew. It is the story of a woman who, in an ir-
responsible moment, starts a big stone rolling
downhill, and vainly calls to it to stop, till
finally it rolls over herself and everybody she
loves, and flattens out her little world into one
general wreck. She is almost the only woman
on record who proved herself an absolutely
reliable shot, with a stone or anything else,
and the results are so tragic that other women
should leave the business alone. The manner
in which Fedora works out her vengeance till it
gets beyond her control, and then repents of
it, and hangs shrieking on to the tail of the
monster she has created, and tries to stop it as
vainly as a speck holding back a Juggernaut, is
exceedingly artistic. It may not be very high art,
but the details of it —such art as it is—are perfect.
* * * *
Mrs. Brough’s Fedora is an excellent crea-
tion, so far as it goes. It would look a lot better
if the public hadn’t seen her agonise in so many
characters and learned her methods, as they learn
everybody’s methods, sooner or later ; if she were
a new actress, and the Russian lady her first
appearance in this country, she would probably
score a triumph. Whether her death-scene is
realistic or not, no one really knows, for, nowa-
an enthusiast in art can’t buy a distracted
female and poison her in a drawing-room to see
how she dies ; but, at all events, it looks as
realistic as anyone could reasonably ask, and the
performance is a graceful and finished one all
through. Titheradge’s Ipanoff is not nearly so
good as many things this accomplished actor has
done. Ward and Miss Faber do the light and
frisky characters admirably, but the police-agent
of Dot Boucicault is the very best of the minor
parts, in his stoniness, and his strict atten-
tion to business, and his cold, stolid, unemotional
readiness to knout anybody on the shortest notice.
If a Russian police-agent doesn’t look exactly like
Boucicault it is his own fault. He certainly ought
to look that way.
* * * *
“Thoroughbred,” which is new to Sydney,
is to be the next production at the Cri., and is due
on 2nd May.
#* * *
A large, harrowing impossibility is the
“ Broad Arrow ’’-so-called, The Bulletin be-
lieves, because ice-cream doesn’t grow in Dahomey
—at Her Majesty’s, Sydney. It is a play in which
incongruity howls at large, and Time and Space
go round together on all-fours and hiccough at
each other on the sea-coast of Bohemia, and co-
herence is out in a state of awful drunk and in-
capable in the region where the green, unripe fact
grows on the rum-shrub, and where all things are
mixed up beyond comprehension. The most as-
tonishing events happen in it in a perfectly friendly
manner, and excite no surprise worth mentioning.
The villain, in the course of a brief but tempestu-
ous career, commits a robbery and gets his brother
fired out for it, embezzles <£20,000 from the same
patient brother when he comes back, gets him
gaoled for stealing his own money, “ blews ” the
principal girl’s fortune, annexes another man’s
wife and has him gaoled also, wrecks a train in
public, partially strangles a lady in evening dress,
and generally wades in gore. He is aided by a
wholly impracticable tunnel—the trains pass the end
of it instead of going through, and it is evidently
meant only for ventilation —and by an unaccount-
able Punch-and-Judy show. The hero takes refuge
in the Punch and J udy, after making his escape
from prison and being shot at and blown up in the
train, and coming down again on a corpse which
proves, by the papers in the pocket, to be that of
his best girl’s long-lost brother who wasn’t pre-
viously mentioned in the drama ; and he goes off
in the presence of the police with the uncouth
entertainment wrapped round him. Then, a
moment later, the next scene reveals him bursting
violently into a friend’s home for refuge, where
the Punch and Judy has somehow arrived in front
of him, through a door that is too small to let it
in, and is resting peacefully in a corner. Finally,
some of the many charges against him are cleared
up by the unsupported statement of the red-
headed drunk, and another accusation is removed
through the assertion of an unauthorised female
that the old convict explained it on his death-bed,
and the deed of gift which was going to cause the
old father s ejection having been found by the
abducted vvife whose previous husband was con-
cealed behind the curtain just before the parent
came in and cast everybody off in the presence of
"J e na, tural result of it all is something
that Ihe Bulletin can’t at present remember.
* * *
The cast mostly requires no particular
mention. Alfred Woods, who makes the best
comic aboriginal in A ustralia, is not at home in
tragic characters. In fact, except Leston and
Robert Inman, no one looks particularly at home.
Ihe leading lady, Miss Lilia Wilde, is good in
spots and very depressing in other spots, and the
rest of the co. seem mostly overwhelmed by the
ridiculousness of the show, and walk through it
apologetically. The railway accident is a large,
red, satisfying incident, and a new light is thrown
on Dartmoor as a place where the convict affably
tells the warder to get his hair cut with impunity,
and that about sums up the drama.
** * *
“ Djin-Djin ” is now in its fourth week at
Sydney Lyceum, and the audience still recalls
Coleman four times after the Scarecrow Dance
with severe regularity. There are many good
items in the panto., but the one which has soaked
deepest into the public soul is evidently the Scare-
crow Dance. Probably this is because Coleman’s
attitude is so suggestive of a defunct preacher who
has been hanged with his belltopper on, and left
there till his black suit grew rusty with exposure,
and the belltopper got bashed down over his ear,
and he became a murky ruin. Also, Coleman’s
dance shows that a scarecrow is practically an old
curate gone to seed, and it explains where the
disused curates go to, and various other matters.
** * *
B. Holt’s great military and bare-legged
spectacle, at Sydney Royal, has undergone no
change, and seems like lasting for a good while
longer. On Friday, it is to be under military
patronage; the local warrior is going to roll up
and look at himself in all the ridiculousness of his
bird-of-paradise get-up, and it is to be hoped the
lesson will do him good.
# * *
The Tivoli show rages with unabated fury.
Alice Leamar has sprung some new songs on the
Sydney public, and La Belle Maie has invented
some new attitudes on her slack wire, and a new
bass person, named Peter J osephs, is in fine voice,
and the general result is satisfactory. Sydney
Music Hall also keeps its head above water, largely
by the aid of a mystic person, named Lazron, who
knocks gravity out in one round, and reduces
Time and Space to general wreck ; but the Opera
House has gone under, and is again the deserted
wreck it used to be.
** * *
“ The Work Girl,” late of Sydney, was let
loose again at Melbourne Royal on Saturday. It
is difficult to trace any connection between this
raw-boned melodrama and the sweating evil
although it wanders into an alleged mantle-factory
m the fourth act. The girls employed at the fac-
tory are a sultry crowd. Their daily toil includes
a couple of comic songs and a rather dissolute
ballet. They sometimes make a pretence of
putting stitches into strips of calico, but, as a rule,
they spend the hours snatched from festivity in
calling the forewoman an “old cat.” The fatted
Hebrew who runs the establishment has no terrors
for them. They tear his top garments ofif his back
previous to hustling him under the table and loot-
ing his trousers, and the curtain falls upon a
tableau of two oppressed mantle-makers flourish-
ing a pair of unmentionables in the face of common
decency. A grave question as to what happens
after this is left open. Probably the victorious
damsels draw lots for the bulk of the Hebrew’s
wardrobe and split up his socks into souvenirs.
* * * *
The hero of “ The Work Girl” is originally
a stone-broke punter, pursued by a heavy villain
who covets his weeping wife. The wife, for her
part, gets overtaken by sudden death, which fatal
incident enables her to re-appear, as her own
daughter, 14 years later, when she loses her heart
to a painful young man, and secures an illicit
baby in exchange. Foolishness seems to run in
the family. Then the hero, having served a
long term of gaol, comes seeking his lost offspring,
and is grieved to find himself a grandfather under
such improper circumstances. All this while a
second villain is chasing his wealthy accomplice
with proofs of a foul murder committed in the
prologue. Finally truth prevails, and the mis-
creant in purple and fine linen comes to grief
along with the evil Genesis who was torn from his
pants. The mother of the unaffiliated infant gets
cleansed of her sin by plunging into the sea of
matrimony, and many other little matters are
cleared up or allowed to die.
*-* * *
The name-part appears to be played by
Miss Florrie Ford, an artiste plucked from the
alls. Anyway, Miss Florrie figures as a work-
girl, of a sort. Her acting is very devoid of detail
but she gives plenty of voice to her lines, and is
associated throughout with a cheerful tripe-dealer
(manager Westmacott). Scot Inglis, Plimmer,
Hazlitt, and J. W. Sweeney hang on to the stray
glimpses of plot with much vigor, whilst Miss
Harrie Ireland impersonates two females in dis-
tress. Miss Ireland, one regrets to observe, is
less sincere in her stage emotions than she used to
be, wherefore, seeing that the young lady has a
fine presence and ample intelligence to work upon
The Bulletin exhorts her to hurry up and do
further credit to her admiring country. Miss
Minna Phillips shines in the minor character of a
sassiety vixen, and the vehicles employed at her
interrupted wedding give pleasure to all.
* * *• *
At the Opera House the last two weeks of
Charles Godfrey are ebbing away, and a benefit
to that sometimes hoarse, but never coarse
comedian is announced. The Waxworks is anxious
to remind country visitors that it still occupies
the same old Bourke-street site, and is always up-
to-date in the Chamber of Horrors. A neat
entertainment by the veteran Cunard happens
there twice daily, for the sake of the surrounding
effigies, as well as the general public. Anything
that relieves the monotony of life for a waxworks
figure deserves liberal support.
** * *
“Uncle Tom’s Cabin,” trimmed with an
interlude performance of trick-cycling, has started
another season at Melb. Alexandra. Dan. Barry’s
edition of the old hallelujah nigger promises to
pay a handsome dividend ; yet if a balloon
ascent, or some fireworks, were added to the pro-
gramme, it might be even more successful.
Thomas, in this instance, is identical with Larry
Dooley, onetime of the Tivoli company, and,
when the aged slave is submitted to auction in a
dress suit, he looks like a corpulent corner-man
mourning thelossof his tambourine. Luckily, Larry
is harmonious, and his portion of some admirable
singing atones for many shortcomings. A good
deal of music and apotheosis embellishes “ Uncle
Tom’s Cabin,” which, in its darkest and most
ferocious moods, is as wallopsome as ever. The
Simon Legree of Augustus Glover earns due
execration from the gallery. Oily Deering, Dan
Barry, Dobson, Miss Ethel Blair, and a valuable
Topsy in Miss Katie Towers, are in the cast, like-
wise twenty more people, blackened or otherwise.
The bloodhounds, “ kindly lent by <£. £. Smith,”
deserve special mention, on account of their age.
These dawgs, or their ancestors, have been
sniffing at lawyer Marks’s legs as long as anybody
can remember, and they serve to remind grey-
haired citizens of days agone, when they were
young men and didn’t feel well.
*# * *
A growing spirit of opposition to Little
Billee manifests itself amidst the “ Trilby ”
boom. The play couldn’t proceed very well with-
out any Billee at all, more’s the pity ; but a hard,
unfeeling'public begins to think that the tiresome
lover might be reduced in quantity. Eor instance,
there is no reason why Billee should bring down
the second curtain with his mechanical cry of
“Tarfy, Tarfy, my hea-a-rt is broken!” If he
were to break his heart in profound silence, signi-
fying the smash by a display of pocket-handker-
chief, the curtain would fall more effectively than
at present. “ Tarfy, Tarfy ”is uncalled for,
and the world disapproves of it when it comes.
Apart from Billee and the Laird the company is
well chosen; the stage management is pictur-
esque, and the conclusion thoroughly artis-
tic. Nothing could be better, in its quiet
way, than Miss Jennie Reiffarth’s discovery
of the heroine’s decease, and her tearful warning
of that sad event to the messieurs in the adjoining
room. True, the man ought not to have rushed
away to afternoon tea, leaving Trilby quite alone,
but no matter. One or two grisettes are becoming
famous for the abandon of their backfalls on the
sofa, and the Christmas Eve revels get giddier all
round. The song off the stage was a comparative
failure last week, owing to the indisposition of
Miss Nagel, whose under-study didn’t rival her
rare, deep notes. However, the original organ is
pealing forth once more.
** * *
Little Billee, unconvincing as he appears,
might have been a much sadder failure, according
to the evidence of men fresh from ’Murka. Two
or three youthful Billees have been tried, with
shocking results. Small-sized mummers, even if
they can act, are not educated in the romantic-
lover line of character. They are comedians—
“low” and otherwise. The trouble is to get a
little man, who looks young and pretty, and can
Eersuade the male portion of an audience to take
im seriously when he talks love. Women are not
so difficult to interest in the matter. Many of
them—especially the big, buxom ones—don’t see
anything funny in a sighing mannikin.
** * *
A concert, for the worthy purpose of en-
larging the Consumptive Sanatorium at Echuca,
will be given in Melb. Town Hall next Tuesday
(28th). The names of Miss Nagel aforesaid, Miss
Maggie Stirling, and a dozen well-known artists
are down upon the programme, also Miss Kings-
ton, a promising young mezzo-soprano, will debut
on this occasion. Just now, when the first breaths
of winter are striking cold upon Melbourne’s
chest, and hacking coughs are a drug on the
market, the Sanatorium concert should appeal to
all sympathetic pockets. Such as are quite in-
different to the good cause may be touched by the
news that Lord Brassey has promised to attend,
and the list of other influential patrons is nearly
as long as the sea-serpent that rules the waves.
** * *
Lalla Miranda has sung to the Parisian
critics, at an invitation concert given by Madame
Richard, her instructress, and the notices predict
a brilliant operatic future for the dark-eyed
damsel. Lalla’s success, when it comes will be a
nasty jar for many Melbourne musical-jargonists
who wouldn’t admit that her sweet voice was
worth a course of training. These weie the people
who gushed over the hard, unsympathetic warble
of Evangeline Florence a few months ago, because
ner meretricious top notes were imported.
