The Wild-Cat Column.
The report of the Mutual Life Association,
though a sound and solvent-looking document, gives some
evidence of the prevailing hard times. The premium in-
come for 1895 is £141,821, against £141,943 in 1894 and
£146,104 in 1893. The new premiums for ’95 (£12,482)
are, however, fully £llOO better than the previous year’s
record, which is a sign of improvement, and the receipts
from interest on investments keep up well, which is
another good feature. The total income for the year is
£195,364, and of this £32,000 has been added to the funds,
making them £1,087,767. Also, the death-losses are very
small.
The total expenses for the year are =£38,114 —
rather over 26| per cent, on premiums, or
per cent, on total income. These rates are dis-
tinctly high, but they are a great improvement on
past figures, and as the improvement is steady and con-
tinuous the Mutual Life Association is obviously moving
in the right direction. The securities have been written
down by £10,721, making no less than £30,843 written off
in the last three years for losses and depreciation. This
process may seem somewhat rough on policy-holders, but
it is the only way to keep the society on an absolutely
secure basis. The report is, in all respects, remarkably
full and distinct. It sets down properties acquired by
foreclosure separately from the society’s office premises
and divides freeholds from leaseholds. Overdue interest
is also a separate item, and the extreme smallness of the
amount (£1117) tends to show that the securities are in a
good condition. Also, there are no items in the list of
assets that are calculated to arouse suspicion, and every-
thing looks solid and prosperous.
Apropos of the want of detail in insurance
balance-sheets. The Mutual of Victoria, like the Mutual
Life Association, shows separately office property, pro-
perty acquired by foreclosure, interest accrued and
interest overdue. It also shows rents separately
from interest, and explains how long the overdue interest
has been outstanding. The A.M.P. Society, the Widows’
Fund, and the Temperance and General also show
interest arrears separately and differentiate between office
property and property acquired by foreclosure ; so that
in these five offices there are at least some definite signs
by which the policy-holders can judge how their funds are
getting on. The City Mutual shows foreclosed properties
separately, but says nothing about outstanding interest.
And the National Mutual and Colonial Mutual both lump
together properties purchased with properties foreclosed
and interest accrued with interest overdue, and give a
minimum of information generally'. Why isn’t there a pre-
scribed form of balance-sheet for all insurance-offices?
* * * *
The Royal Bank (Melb.) shows a slight improve-
ment on its previous balance-sheet. The net profits for
the half-year ended 31st March were £2099, against
£2038 for the previous half, and £2136 and £2875 for the
two preceding ones. Said profit equals about £1 Bs. per
cent, per annum, so the question of div. is again left in
abeyance, but the balance to credit of profit and loss now
represents £19,477. Deposits show an increase of £3OOO ;
bills in circulation, an increase of nearly £13,000 ; bills
receivable are £24,000 better, and note circulation has
gone up by £500; and these figures, combined with
the first improvement shown in the profits for a lom>-
time, suggest that things are on the mend with theßoyaf.
The available cash (£89,981) equals nearly 35 per cent, of
the liabilities to the public, which is a remarkably sub-
stantial reserve, and, as the uncalled capital is equal to
nearly double the liabilities, the position of the depositors
is sufficiently good. The directors remark sadly that the
Companies Act hasn’t been passed, and consequently they
are still waiting for an Opportunity to write off lost capi-
tal and put things on an absolutely straight footing, which
remark has now appeared in at least four successive
reports, and is beginning to grow wearisome. And The
Bulletin again suggests, as a simple solution, that the
Royal should write-off its accumulated balance of £19,447
as an instalment, at all events, and put the remainder of
the losses to the debit of profit and loss or to suspense
account, and so put things as straight as it can, in the
meantime. Surely the shareholders have a right to know
how they stand.
* * * *
The Union Trustee Co. of Melbourne has
decided not to pay any dividend this half-year. The net
profits for the 12 months are only £555, against £3648 last
year, and £2797 the one before, which is a very depressim* 1
state of things. The co. has £67,000 of its own money
invested (£12,420 in 4 per cent, debentures, £22,400 in 3i
per cent, debentures, £32,451 on mortgage, and the
balance on fixed deposit), and the interest totals about
£2970, estimating the mortgages at only 5 per cent.
Therefore it would seem that this year the trustee business
has been a dead loss of £2400 ; and the last two years, on
an average, it paid just a shade over working expenses.
The trustee business, in short, has been overdone in Mel-
bourne, where five companies are trying to live on an in-
dustry which, in Sydney, only manages to support two. In
August last the Union Trustee Co. rashly paid an interim
dividend of 2 per cent, which absorbed £1476, or
fully 800 more than the year’s net earnings, therefore
the balance to credit of profit and loss is reduced from
£2583 to £1662 as the result of a year’s work. The Union
Co., though it has been about 11 years in existence, has
no reserve fund; its shares (20s. paid-up) are selling
at about 6s. 6d. ; and its profits for the past year (which
amount to a little over 15s. per cent.) are not calculated to
make a, boom. There are no shady-looking features
in the balance-sheet; liberal reserves have been made
against possible losses, and apparently the co. is sound
and well-managed. The only trouble is a great deal too
much trustee co. in proportion to population.
* * * *
Russell-Jones, M.L.A. (N.S.W.), has a Bill on
hand providing for the absolute protection of life-insur-
ance policies from creditors, with the limitation that, if
the assured goes bankrupt or dies within three years after
insuring, the creditors shall have a lien on the' policy for
the amount of the premiums paid with 6 per cent, in-
terest. Tiie Bulletin strongly recommends that this last
proviso be cut out, and the Bill be made to read simply
that all policies up to £5OOO, and on which the premium
is payable quarterly, half-yearly, or annually, be abso-
lutely protected from everybody. It is impossible to pro-
tect all policies for any amount, else the prospective
insolvent might take out an endowment on his own life
tor £50,000, pay down the premium out of his creditors’
money in one lump of £25,000 or so, and then go broke
with this comfortable nest-egg put away, but the £5OOO
limit with the above proviso seems good enough. The
proposal of Jones, M.L.A., will, in many cases, defeat
itself. If a man goes insolvent three years after insuring
tor, say, £lOOO (premium, say, £4O annually), what
amount of protection is it which makes him owe £l2O and
£l4 Bs. interest on a policy whose present cash-value is
probably £6O ? At that rate, it would be £7B Bs. cheaper
lor , , ® protected one if the creditors seized the policy
and left him to start a fresh assurance.

Melbourne Argus says, regarding certain
recent bank-smashes, that no criminality attached
to any of the directors, because none of them
feathered their own nests with the vanished assets
Which is ridiculously bad logic, whether these particular
directors were criminal or not. First, no man knows
whether another man has feathered his own nest or not.
Second, if a man takes money he shouldn’t have taken,
the fact that he lost it afterwards in foolish speculations
doesn’t alter the case at all. If a clerk embezzles £lOO
from his employer, what difference does it make whether
he puts it in the savings-bank or “ blews ” it on a horse-
race? Does anyone ever claim that the embezzling clerk
isn’t criminal because he lost the money ? The directors
in question may have been honest enough in their inten-
tions, but, if the Argus supports them much longer in
this fashion, they won’t look like it.
* * * *
N.S.W. Govt., after much inexplicable delay
and shuffling, is at last refunding the civil-servants the
amounts of their contributions to the Superannuation
Fund. This refers only to those who resigned since the
Act was amended, i e., at the beginning of this year.
Those who resigned before then cannot claim, and won’t
get a solitary copper. Those who still continue in the
service are permitted, under the amended Act, to cease
contributing to the Fund ii they feel so inclined. Many
have felt that way, and have withdrawn, with thanks,
from a fund in which they never knew exactly how they
stood for two consecutive days. But, all the same, those
who have withdrawn find that they are not entitled to a
refund of their money until they resign, when, they are
informed, they can claim the total amount of their con-
tributions, with 3 p.c. added—the interest, though, to be
calculated only from the beginning of this year.
* * • *
Much of the legal aspect of the Mirams libel-
case hano-s round the meaning of the phrase “ to play
ducks and drakes.” The Slang Dictionary and sundry
copies of the “ Notes and Queries” have been searched
on the subject. Webster gives a good definition also :
“ To play at ducks and drakes with property—to throw it
away heedlessly or squander it foolishly and unpro-
fltably.”

The most deplorable feature of present Aus-
tralian unionism—shearers, coalminers, and trades—is
that it is a house visibly divided against itself. It tries to
cast out devils by Beelzebub, the prince of the devils.
* * * *
The secretary of the Vie. Customs has received
a report from the inspector of drawbacks re a statement
copied by the London Citizen (without acknowledgment)
from the disreputable Bulletin. The report in question
practically confirms the truth of this beastly journal’s
much-quoted paragraph. The inspector says : “There is
no doubt that advantage has been taken by merchants of
the reduced rates of duty which have recently come into
operation to ship considerable quantities of old stock, in
order to obtain drawback on the old rates, but I know of
no instance in which goods have been shipped for draw-
back which have become useless or rotten, or since the
present regulations, of ‘ less value for house use than the
amount of drawback claimed ’ ; but, previous to that, it
had been the practice for some time, though not in ac-
cordance with the Act, to allow exporters (more particu-
larly soft-goods merchants) to obtain drawback on articles-
which have been found unsaleable at the end of a season,
at a price equal to the amount of duty originally paid.
As on goods not sold, but shipped for the supply of a
branch house, obviously only the full amount of duty
paid can be claimed, and the original invoice stamped by
a landing-waiter must be produced, I fail to see how the
assertion that invoices are ‘shockingly untruthful’ can
apply.” But The Bulletin doesn’t fail to see that old,
decrepit goods can he adapted to the requirements of an
original invoice that referred to quite a different lot. To
the Customs swindles of the psalm-singing soft-goods trade
there is no end.
« * * *
Dear Bulletin, —You say it is illogical to assume
that the counterfeit-silver-coinage trouble is just begin-
ning. It is a recent trouble, because the slump in silver
is recent. Last week’s letter assumes that profitable
counterfeiting can be done while silver remains below 40
pence per ounce —leaving a minimum of 16 pence per
ounce for expenses and profit. Silver never got below 40
pence till 1892, when it averaged 39 13-16, in 1893 it was
35%, in 1894 it dropped to 29, and it would be about 30
for 1895. This indicates a period of four years to conceive,
develop, and carry out a big swindle necessitating secrecy,
study, and organisation.—M. [The conception probably
occupied thirty seconds. Secresy doesn’t occupy much
time. Any man who had ever worked in a mint had all
the study ready to his hand, and when the coiner makes
as good an article as the State there isn’t much need for
organisation. The development comes by itself if the
swindle proves profitable. Allow three months for the
whole lot. —Ed. B.j

The New Manly Ferry Co. holds out what the
public will regard as a signal of distress by announcing
that the return fare is to be raised to 6d. The old co.
simultaneously advertises that it will maintain the 3d.
fare. Before the 3d. fare era Manly was a good place to
live in ; now it is only a place to die in. It is the place to
which suicides now appear to resort. The sea-shore, lay
the way, seems to have a strong attraction even for
suicides whose modus is not drowning.

* * » *
The thing that now strikes a'stranger hardest in
Coolgardie is the utter absence of anything tending to
the belief that Coolgardie is the centre of (as its admirers
contend) the biggest goldfield on earth. There is no
sound of stampers ; no noise from, any of the machinery
at the mines round ; no machinery being dragged through
its streets; no lucky diggers about spending their
“boodle”; in short, no evidence that any of the mines
that have got machinery are working, or that any gold is
being won from the earth.
* * * *
“ I visited Bayley’s the other day,” says a Bul-
letin correspondent. “It was a glorious, sunshiny
morning, and the walk to the mine, about a mile from the
P. 0., was delightful, although one missed the foliage and
bird-life of most other parts of Australia. At the mine,
everything was quiet as the grave, the claim having been
shutdown for some time. The externals—the battery,
machinery, buildings—are on the most lavish scale;
fine draught horses are feeding quietly in the stable on
the best of hay, and the manager’s fowls, clucking about
his well-built and neat homestead, are as good as money
can purchase. Bayley’s seems to have everything a mine
should possess, except—gold.”
* * *
*
The London Mount Morgan people, D’Arcy,
Tom Hall, &c., have formed a syndicate for the purpose of
dealing in Australian mines. Their first operation, the
floating of the Mount Ferruin (W.A.) mine, was a tremen-
dous success, the thing being fairly rushed. Though a
great many of the companies lately advertised have gone
to allotment, the public (writes The Bulletin’s corres-
pondent) has been chary of taking a hand, and the under-
writing co's have been stuck lo the extent of 90 per cent.
It is time they began to disgorge their unholy gains, for
they had a good innings when first the Westralian boom
set in. When are the long-expected Westralian divs.
coming ? A few good declarations would be most welcome
just now, as everyone is predicting a revival of interest in
mining, which only needs the stimulus of a few divs. to
set it going. But there will be an awful shaking-out in
connection with the countless bubble co’s floated in
London.
* * * «
A most reliable mining anatomist —and from his
characteristic letter many will identify him—writes from
Kalgoorlie to The Bulletin : —“ I was down to see the
bottom development in the Boulder. They had driven
on a footwall stringer, and had to cross-cut 15 feet to the
reef which they had followed from the 150 ft. level in a
winze. The reef is about 12ft. wide, and similar material
to that mined at the 150 ft. level. They would not let me
sample it, but I could see it was being sent to the mill.
Lake View has also cut the lode at 200 ft., where it was
the full width of the shaft, and the manager told me it
sampled from 3oz. to 6oz. In both mines the reef in the
lower levels is living in the hard country, and I do not
think either will cut out; but the soft nature of the lode
material may change at any time to hard, and then the
question if the gold lives with depth will be settled. In
the Brown Hill, the vein formation continues in depth,
but it is hard and compact, with a large percentage of fine
sulphides and no gold. It is claimed that the shoot of ore
is south of the present lower developments.”
“ All the other mines on the south end which
have been developed have the same character of reefs.
You can notice by their workings that they have a fear of
the mines in depth. In the Boulder Main Reef, in par-
ticular, the shaft is 90ft. deep, but the lode is opened up
at 60ft., and shows a fine body of ore for several hundred
feet. I understand this has been opened some time, but
they have not put down a winze, which, I should say,
would suggest itself, as the vein-formation is soft and easy
sinking ground.
“The milling plants, and their lame attempts
to get a supply of water, together with a lack of
developments in depth, go to show that the Australian
owners are out for a sale of stock this summer in the
London market.
“I have an idea that the mines will live at
depth, but the shoots of ore will be shorter. However
that may be, there is no question as to the wonderful
developments of high-grade ore near the surface, and, if
the owners were half as good miners as the reefs are good
mines, the country, or rather the district, would be
further advanced, and be in a position to produce large
returns this winter.
“ The Brown Hill mill is not a big success. It
consists of two ore-crushers and a large set of rolls, by
which it was proposed to reduce the ore to a fineness or
40 mesh. From the rolls it is conveyed to a bin, thence
to an amalgamating barrel, revolved for two hours with a
light solution of cyanide and a charge of quicksilver,
thence the pulp passes over quicksilver traps to recover
the quicksilver and amalgam, and the pulp is run into
vats for cyanide. The original plan called for amalgama-
tion copper plates in the barrels to recover the coarse
gold, but these have been discarded. The fact that they
have no settlers after the barrels to regain the quicksilver
I believe will cause trouble. From trial tests they have
recovered up to 87 per cent, on lOoz. ore by the barrel and
light solution of cyanide. The oil engines I understand
have so far given satisfaction, but their crushing plant is
working very badly, yet it cannot be said they have had a
really fair test.
“ On the north end of the district, outside of
Hannan’s Reward, there is no improvement except that
they have the same small rich stringers and big lodes of
decomposed formation with stringers of quartz, which in
its decomposed state, may give 8 or l(klwts. per to
TTunmn’s Reward may be a good mine, but I fear it will
prove at the 300 ft. a large mass of diorite and stringers of
nuartz All who have visited Menzies speak well of it,
but no one is allowed to examine the bottom levels
Question ? The other sections of the entire field are very
quiet. The old story—too much white quartz^
Bavlev’s has still a market-value of i! 125,000.
Nearlv all the shares are now held in London and la ns.
Havimr bought at £1 and upwards, it takes some time for
J. Bulf and J. Frog to realise that they are not worth a
bob. , , *

Another rich strike made in the Croesus mine at
Hannan’s. This makes at least 50 rich finds in two years,
and as it only has had one or two crushings the pur-
chasing English Co., which continues to put up deposits,
should complete the deal and pay itself back by
dollying out the great hunks of gold lying promiscuously
about the mine, and with the balance pay some big divs.
After that it would be a good idea to get some more gold
out of the mine.

A question interesting to brokers and scrip-
ownersarising out of the alleged conversion by a firm of
sharebrokers of 100 Shenandoah shares was discussed at
great length in Melb. County Court last week. The
negotiability of scrip may depend on the final decision in
this case, as the sharebrokers allege that there is a
“custom” of negotiability in connection with mining
scrip sufficiently strong to relieve them from the liability
of what might otherwise be conversion.

At a M.L. meeting of creditors it was found
that there were no assets in the estate. “ Well,” said one
of the creditors, “give us a song, Blank. Let us have
something for our brass.” The impecunious one rose up
and sang “ Then you’ll remember me.”

* * * *
E.D. : “A recent Wild Cat par. asserts: ‘ln
nine cases out of ten, a manager leaving a mine is prima
facie evidence that it should be left by the shareholders
as well.’ Perhaps—when the manager leaves voluntarily;
but my experience teaches that in nine cases out of ten
when a manager leaves a mine, it is because a director up
town wants to put his wife’s cousin on in his place, or
because the manager is a man of grit, and refuses to find
a job in the face for some yahoo who doesn’t know a pick
from a drill, but who is a constituent of a political direc-
tor ; or because he has presumed to correct the impression
of the bombastic director that the underlay has something
to do with the bob-pit. Anyhow, nine cases out of ten,
it is through the fatuous meddlesomeness of the chuckle-
headed city mining man, who has acquired his experience
in the bacon-trade or peddling scrip. Directors of mines
are usually appointed for the reason that would induce
people to set Esquimaux growing orchids —because they
know nothing whatever about the business.”
Again the old contrast between Bunda-
berg and Mackay. Bundaberg, in Glassey’s
victory, emphasised a preference for the cause of
white Queensland and Democracy. Mackay
meekly returned the Boodlewraithian representa-
tives, and took the Kanaka, the Jap., the Chow
and all the leprous crew once more to its foetid
bosom. The Bundaberg victory is mainly due to
the fact that the little town possesses some of the
most ardent Rads and Labor propagandists in all
Queensland. Two or three men, by constant
endeavor and sacrifice, have made possible the
recent victory.
Constable Gordon, of Bendigo, probably
regrets the hour when he got married. The other
day a farmer named Northby wrote to Mrs.
Gordon imploring her to elope w ith him, and
Gordon—reckoning this a chance to n:a ;e a capture
and achieve promotion in the foorce—sent back a
telegram in his wife’s name,-asking giddy Northby
to call at the house after dark. When Northby,
full of sinful hope, came up toscratch, the ingenious
husband beat him brutally and dragged him into
custody for having unlawfully been on his
(Gordon’s) premises. It was a very scriking in-
stance of a policeman’s belief in his special licence
to do as he damn well pleases when he feels
aggrieved. However, thecase against farmer North-
by, who endured one broken nose and innumer-
able bruises, was dismissed. The constable is the
underneath dawg just now. He will take his trial
for assault and robbery, the allegation being that
he “ shook ” from the pocket of his wife’s damaged
admirer a sum of =£7B, which the amorous fellow
had brought along for elopement purposes. The
case is unique. Supposing that a husband does
so take money, is it a vulgar theft or a virtuous
transaction in defence of his wife’s “ honor ’’ ? A
man who has been cleaned out can’t conveniently
elope with a woman, anyway. Also, she, for her
part, loses all desire to roam if her lawful husband
lays hold upon the other fellow’s capital. Money
is a powerful persuader. It can either make the
mare to go astray, or tempt her to remain in the
stable.
The one subject for discussion in Sydney
at time of writing was whether Premier Reid did
the square and honorable and gentlemanly thing
in visiting Parkes on what looked then like his
death-bed, and condoling with him. The sight of
Reid was probably more likely to inflame Parkes
—if the old, ear-chewing warrior was in anything
like his usual fighting condition—and fill him with
smothered rage than the sight of any other man on
this earth, and the visit was calculated either to
hasten the old man’s death or else to rouse him tc
a desperate resolve to recover and do something
dreadful. Also, as Parkes was helpless at the
time, and too weak even to use violent language,
he couldn’t very well eject or shoo away Reid,
eaen if he wished to, so the situation was
somewhat like that of the hangman who first
ties up his victim so that he can’t stoush
him or kick him, and puts the white cap on his
head, so that he can’t see to butt, and then
proceeds to shake hands with him. On the
other hand, if Reid hadn’t visited Parkes,
and Parkes had died, the genial, fuzzy man with
the eye-glass would have been taunted ever after-
wards with being unable to forgive his enemy,
even on his deathbed. He might, indeed, have
struck a middle course by leaving his card surrep-
titiously at the front door, but that would have
looked as if he didn’t want to say anything kind
to his opponent, and preferred merely to stand off
and infuriate him by reminding him that he (Reid)
was alive and well, and in possession of Parkes’
beloved old billet and enjoying himself thoroughly,
while his predecessor was sick and stone-broke
and altogether down in the world. On the whole,
there is room for an interesting debate on the
question : What should Reid have done when
Parkes was ill in order to show good-feeling and
yet give Parkes a decent chance to recover ?

A brilliant suggestion for the re-naming of
South Australia comes from Lieut.-Colonel Ber-
gusson. This loyal warrior thinks that the province
should adopt the title of Kingsland for two reasons.
H would not only balance well with Queensland,
but would recall the fact that the colony was
founded in the reign of King William the Fourth,
after whose Queen its fair capital was named.”
I ergusson overlooks one important point. ‘ ‘ Kings-
land ” would become a vague, uncertain term as
time went on; its historical significance would
fadeaway, so to speak. Now William IY. was
popularly known as Silly Billy, and a billy is an
article of everyday Australian use. Why not con-
vert South Australia into Sillybillyland, thus
emphasising the appropriateness of its capital
city’s nomenclature ?
* * * *
Gov. Brassey indulged in wild vagaries on
Easter Sunday, at Langwarrin Encampment.
After church service, H.E. mounted his fiery steed
and careered wildly around camp unattended,
after the manner of a middy ashore. One hand
held on his extraordinary pith helmet, while the
other grasped the reins, and the tails of his clerical-
looking coat streamed aft in the wind. Every-
where he flew guards turned out, much to the dis-
comfiture of the local Tommy Atkins, who was
feeding at the time. A.D.C. Shaftesbury contem-
plated the scene from afar and swore softly but
earnestly.

Vice-Chancellor M‘Laurin’s speech, at
to be delivered without interruption.
Writes*a Sydney University man*:—
The trouble between the Senate and the students of the
University is amusing enough, but, with double murders
and sensational suicides to hand, the daily papers need
hardly have given it such prominence. The Senate, how-
ever, has really only itself to blame. When, in the past,
the noise and interruptions became disgracefully promi-
, me authorities did not put a peremptory stop to the
display of hoodlumism. Instead, they suggested to that
committee which they now refuse to recognise that
?° n £ s should be arranged by the students. The
idea was that concentrated, organised noise would
be better than scattered interruption. They not only
countenanced the singing of songs but actually encour-
aged it. They found a place for the songs on the othcial
programme and even consulted the students a ‘
positions on the programme they would like for t
songs. Having made this compromise, degrad ng as it
was, they can hardly expect that the students wi q -
allow them to stop the disturbance which they
hitherto fostered. One effect of the noise at Co
ration was to do away with the speech by the Chancellor.
For many years this speech has been pnnted jiiit not
delivered'. But this year the absence of Judge W indejer
gave the Vice-Chancellor an opportunity of which ne was
determined to avail himself.
BOTH SIDES OF THE QUESTION.
BENEVOLENT OLD HUBBY : “ Dear, dear, look
at that poor young thing. Isn’t marriage
a terrible lottery?”
PRETTY YOUNG WIFE : “It is—so very few of
us can hope to be widows at thirty !”
When Gov. Smith visited Coolgardie, dur-
ing the great railway-drunk-celebration, the
Afghan residents presented him with a camel.
That aggravating animal smells very much like a
stale football which has been left in the sink for
three weeks, and it is also a loud, fretful, bad-
tempered beast, which will want to bite the Smith
family at the first opportunity, and probably Gov.
Smith doesn’t want to go about Perth on a camel,
for fear of rousing the small boys of the neighbor-
hood. But, being a Governor, it is his business
to smile under all circumstances, so he accepted
the hairy incubus and went for a ride on it, and
got so severely concussed that he probably won’t
feel quite the same Smith for a long time. He
also offered up Lady Smith as a sacrifice by bump-
ing her round the town on the animal; and that
bruised and aching lady expressed her gratitude.
Some of the things that a Governor’s wife has to
look as if she was grateful for are pretty hard
things.
Miss DAISIE COP PIN.

specialist was on his trial for manslaughter, and a
lot of allegedly - cured cancerous persons were
sworn as witnesses. They all kissed the san?»

the other day, left a letter saying that she intended
killing herself because her husband kept “ study-
ing the Scriptures and smoking his pipe, whilst
his house and children were eaten up with fleas.”

AN APPLICATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the great storm in Melbourne, the other Sunday, a
young woman, while running for shelter to a church, was
killed by the fall of a hoarding. Her name was Love.
Melb.
So, while we live,
Come storm and stress
With suddenness,
Nor warning give ;
And in the whirlwind even
Love
Is overtaken.
And Faith, grown weak,
Storm-driven fails
At altar-rails,
A port to seek,
And seeing only clouds above
Is rudely shaken.
But though the sky
Breathe storm and stress
With suddenness,
Love cannot die ;
The earthly death is but the
portal
To Life immortal.
H. C. C.

Agent for a certain Australian
office canvassing Mick Moloney to
assure his son’s life. Moloney states
that he has promised to put son in
the A.M.P. Society. “ What ! ”
says the agent ; “ you, an Irishman
and a Catholic, put your son into
the Australian Mutual Protestant So-
ciety !” “Sure, then,” says Mick
“ an’ it’s a Protestant society it is
Thin by the powers he can go in wid
you.” And he did. Nowadays an
agent has to be equal to any emer-
gency.
* * «
Acting - Judge Rogers, while
hearing a N.S.W. criminal case the
other day, stopped to send for some
cough-lozenges for a juror with a bad
cold. When the juror had solemnly
munched them, and announced that
he felt “ much better, thanks,” the
case proceeded. There are many ill-
nesses that disturb the course of
N.S.W. justice, and it’s a pity that
J udge Rogers can’t be made Supreme
Court physician -in - ordinary. A
little prescription from him for a fel-
low-judge’s liver, or for an uncon-
scious bias derived from a deranged
kidney, or for a tendency to hang-
by-the-neck-till-dead caused by a
slight lesion in the left cerebral
hemisphere, might be productive of
the very best results.
* * *
The other day Q. Govt, ukased
that the “ walk-about Kanaka ” —the
slave who tires of his bondage at the
expiry of his agreement, and “ walks
about ” for a holiday, keeping up
the market price of his labor—must
go to work after one month or go to
gaol. The planters fondly hoped that
this ruse would make the “ boys ” who
held out for .£1 a week come pleading
for ss. ; but the boys, developing un-
expected spirit and dignity, vow they
will sooner go home to their islands.
A large number of “ cheap and reli-
ables ” left Bundaberg per Rio Loge
the other day, and many more are
waiting to go by the Helena. And
“ the sugar industry is being ruined ”
once more, because it really looks as
if the planters would have to employ
a few whites at 15s. a week—poor
whites ! —for six months of the year.
* * *
At the big Melb. anti-sweating
meeting, amongst the orators Deakin
took the laurel for manner, Cham-
pion for matter. Deakin has the ad-
vantages of a fine voice and a striking
presence, and at times he has a melo-
dramatic touch that reaches the mil-
lion. Champion deals out excellent
material, and does not hesitate to
sting his audience, but he lacks fire-
works. The Dismal Jimmy of the
meeting was a Ballarat delegate—a
prattling parson who gassed limply for half-an-
hour and bored the meeting to the verge of frenzy
with personal prosing and “miner” details,
“ Beware of parsons ! ” is a good motto for persons
arranging such meetings.

Kerosene Bay, with the throat cut. The pair had
lived unhappily, and Down had been m gaol for
assaulting her, but she visited lnm regularly
during his imprisonment. The woman was no
doubt murdered by her husband- anyhow she was
murdered, for when did a woman commit suicide
by throat-cutting ? Down was 60 years old and Ins
wife wasn’t. She was a woman with attractive
eyes and a beautiful head of hair, and, like him,
she drank occasionally. «
Down was a disappointing individual. When
the tragedy was first reported it was announced
that he was heir to <£60,000, which vaguely sug-
gested that he was tall and young and adorned
with a beautiful moustache like an heir in the
Family Herald ; also, that he drove to South
Head in a cab and gave all his money to the cab-
man —which had a lordly sound about it—before
he threw himself over. People pictured him as
the stately hero out of the London J ournal, who,
being disappointed in love, threw a bag of gold to
the cabby and disappeared in a poetic fashion over
the cliff. Then it gradually transpired that he
was a retired sausage-man who used to fight
desperately with his wife in a cheap boarding-
house, and that his expectations only amounted to
<£4ooo, and that he was a common, violent, three-
penny-beer person with no special picturesqueness
about him at all. The last time he and his wife
were seen together she was removing him out of a
pub. because they had to go and look for new
lodgings, having got notice to quit for dragging
each other round the old lodgings by the hair. The
more or less demented sausage-man seems to have
sawn his wife’s throat suddenly in the midst of
the search for new lodgings.
* * * *
Nothing new under the sun. It is re-
marked, as a wonder, in Melb., that a woman
claiming to be a qualified seafaring person has
applied for a mate’s certificate. Mrs. Taylor’s
book on navigation is a standard, and she com-
manded a ship for many voyages.
* * * *
That enterprising young woman who
wanted to be examined for a second mate’s certifi-
cate hadn’t a sea-leg to stand on, because she’d
have had to pass in practical seamanship—involv-
ing a knowledge of work aloft, where it doesn’t
appear she’d ever been. Also, if, as she threatens,
she attempts to obtain the certificate as a man,
she will have touched the law at two points
making a false declaration and wearing “ drag.”
Disguised female foremasters there have been,
and will be; but the Brute will still keep the
quarterdeck for himself.
* * * * *
A scandal—arising out of the alleged
tampering by an official of a semi-Govt. institu-
tion with the Board-election ballot-papers—will,
in all probability, soon be heard of in the law-
courts.

No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, and in no case will MS. be
returned unless stamps (of any province) accompany.

It is laid down in the principal Merchant Shipping book,
although apparently a case has never been decided, that
a sailor, when met by the plea of desertion in an action
for his wages, may recover if he can show that the captain
wanted to put to sea in an unseaworthy ship. The reason-
ing is based on two sections of the Merchant Shipping
Act, one of which provides that the seaworthiness of the
ship is implied in any contract with the sailor J.R. :
Our advice—sell out E.S.V. : Next week Bundle-

A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION. /
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ;9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
mam walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long. #

Sydney, April 21, 1896.
My Dear Moorabinda —
If the numberless engagements and rumors of
engagements credited to a certain well-known
handsome society girl now having the necessary
clothes made in which to really and truly face the
matrimonial music, and no shilly-shally—if these
alleged conquests have any real foundation, the
happy man must feel something like Mrs. Blue-
beard felt when she peeped in and saw all the
scalps. Evidently the bouncing damsel regards
the aforesaid scalps in a philosophical light, for
the new “ engage,” which threatens to end in a
gold hoop, was in the air a few days after the
demise of one youth who looked all over a favorite
for a whole season. However, it really seems that
the lady is suited at last.
Pretty Grace Palotta hasn’t been lost in London,
but was playing at the Gaiety Theatre in the
“Shop Girl,” taking the part of Lady Dodo, as
she did here, and singing the same song, “Over
the Hills.” Palotta also took part in “All
Abroad,” in which she had a song, “ The Business
Girl, ” when she changed her dress four times in
full view of the audience, winding-up as a be-
witching “ boy,” in a pale-blue satin suit. From a
Gaiety co. standpoint, pale-blue satin pants are
very promising things in which to ascend the
ladder of fame, and may be regarded as a flag of
triumph, metaphorically speaking. Writing to a
Sydney friend, Miss Palotta enthuses over
blonde Ethel Haydon as one of the prettiest and
most charming of girls. Judging by Miss
Palotta’s letter, that Sydney pressman’s chance
looks brighter. “ Madge Rossel and I often talk
of the good times we had in Australia, and hope
they will return some day.”
High-toned damsels voyaging to Australia per
gilded mail-boat often get their finer feelings
grievously wrenched and their inherent decollet-
age, as it were, seriously trampled upon by the
lucky Coolgardians who come aboard at Westralia,
and who never have their willie-wagtails along,
but dine in large checks, flannel shirts and
other abominations. They generally get a table
to themselves far from the bare collar-bones and
boiled shirts of the society elemeilt, who exchange
looks of petrified astonishment and reach out for
their scent-bottles.
But it was left to an Australienne recently en
route from London, where she had got the indis-
pensable amount of British rudeness top-thatched
over her education, to really put these dreadful
creatures in their right place. A party of more or
less lucky Coolgardians of good class boarded the
steamer. They had no dress clothes, but they
kept to themselves at a table as usual, and after
dinner one of them struck a few home-sick chords
on the piano. This was the last straw to their
compatriot, who, at the sight of a serge suit on
a piano-stool after 9 p.m., was overcome by her
outraged feelings. She rose abruptly. “Ai shall
never touch the piano again after those Coolgardie
savages,” she said, indignantly, and swept scorn-
fully to her cabin. However, one of the savages
got even with the lady at the end of the journey
by proposing a clever toast which everybody
listened to and understood, while the damsel
turned every color in the beet-root family.
I believe there are certain sceptics who profess
to believe that the New Woman is merely the
Mrs. ’Arris of journalisin, and has no local habi-
tation, despite the bloomers in the shop windows,
the cigarette-smoking females on the walls, and the
bicycle. But surely they must swallow her now
when, in Victoria, she is applying for the position
of mate, and threatening to dress up as a man
and go ’Ome to get her certificate and end as a
captain, so there.
I have often wondered why the new female
didnk tackle the sea long ago, if only for the gold
buttons on the captain’s costiime, and his undis-
puted right to have the last word and go on the
bridge whenever he likes, and pick and choose the
company at his end of the table. A lawyer once
told me that, in Equity, sailors, women, and
lunatics are all classed together, and are dealt
gently with, as more or less irresponsible persons ;
so it looks as if the newest woman in Victoria has
a good case in trying to turn into a sailor, with
whom she is already on equal terms legally. She
can also turn into a lunatic at the same time, if
she likes. But bloomers should be insisted upon.
A company of amateurs is putting up “H.M.S.
Pinafore ” at Sydney Gaiety on April 29th and
May 2nd, in aid of the funds of the North Shore
Hospital. There are rumors that Gov. Hampden
will be present on one evening, and, as the ama-
teurs in question play ‘ ‘ Pinafore ” smartly and
have done it all before, the performances will,
doubtless, result in a big hat.
A young lady rose in the east in Sydney recently
and gave a piano-recital “ prior to her departure
for England.” She seemed to be an excellent
pupil, but her fireworks were scarcely brilliant
enough to justify her going ’Ome on them. But,
then, everybody who has a mere liking for any of
the arts rushes Europewards to aggravate it and
tease it until, after many weary years, it ends in a
big brass plate and one small pupil. Piccadilly,
alas, is paved with brilliant pianists, who will play
you a dozen sdjiatas and a fugue thrown in almost
for a square ineal. So say the long-haired men
who try to live by art in Sydney.
Bland Holt’s new leading woman, Miss Eliza-
beth Watson, is very like her sister Henrietta,
though not so swan-necked and nothing like so
good an actress. Her taste in dress is not so un-
usual, and there is much more method in her mil-
linery. In “ One of the Best ” she wears a stylish
green gown with black points and her neck bare
(bare necks would seem to run in that family).
Then a smart yellow satin gown with brocade
shoulder-flaps and frill sleeves of yellow chiffon ;
then a Quaker gown of grey; and lastly broad
black and white stripes, black and white hat en
suite, and a fluffy parasol. The other new Thes-
pienne, Miss Prances Ross, wears her chestnut
hair over her ears, and is a gracefully pretty woman
with good taste in dress and a voice that cracks
under emotional pressure. Miss Ross plays the
grig aspect of Mary Penrose admirably, but her
appeal to the Court-Martial to spare Walter
Baker wouldn’t have moved the heart of a nurse-
girl. It must be rather awful to be born an
ingenue and know that you must giggle and flip a
lace handkerchief down the theatrical years if you
want to be a success.
A soothing thing about “One of the Best” is
that you see more of the Bland Holts in it than
/anybody ever saw before—more of Mrs. Holt, at
least, for the genial Bland, in kilts, doesn’t come
within coo-ee of the rose-pink Bland, simply
draped in a bath-towel, whom Mrs. Holt discovers,
bathing, in the “Ratal Card.” But Mrs. Holt in
knickers is a pleasing revelation—from a front
view, anyhow. “ One of the Best ”is a coherent
and probable play, and, if only Cosgrove’s hair
wouldn’t lie down so aggressively and refuse to
arise even in a struggle, it would be difficult to
find fault with the gorgeous and lavish production.
“ One of the Best ” certainly is a “ cleamy ” play,
as Saturday’s programmes admitted. I have no
idea what sort of a play a ‘ ‘ cleamy ” play is—but
it is always safest to agree with the management.
The “Djin-Djin ” matinee last Saturday had a
tight-fit audience, and travelled from 2 to 5 with
the smoothness begotten of three weeks’ run.
There is no dust on the hem of Djin-Djin’s gar-
ments so far, and, while the golden ballet con-
tinues to look like new pins, and no harm comes
to Miss Young’s legs, there is no telling when the
large scintillating production will pull up. By the
way, Plo. Young ought to cork her speaking
visage and reduce her wardrobe if she really wants
to look like Prince Eucalyptus. All the royalties
of Australia are as black as the ace of spades, and
wear a great deal less than even Miss Young does.
A convincing Prince Eucalyptus who would bring
Flora Graupner to grass with a boomerang, and
feed her with raw ’possum out of a dilly-bag,
would lend much variety to the dazzling scene
when the paint begins to wear off.
A praiseworthy point about Mrs. Brough’s
Fedora was that she had not forgotten the dress-
maker, for the long-tailed wardrobe of the latest
version suffered nothing by comparison with other
Fedoras, and was miles ahead of the gaudy,
trumpery things Achurch had a fashion of ban-
daging her figure in. In the first act a paletot of
brocade with deep hems of sable and black velvet
sleeves was thrown off to show an ivory brocade
with bodice of shot tobacco-brown silk. In the
second a • very artful mingling of heliotrope
brocade and black, the bodice criss-crossed in
black jet lines ; in the third, a sallow tea-
gown with suggestions of green jet ; and in
the fourth frock Mrs. Brough looked best
of all —it was black silk broken up with
gold-spangled passementerie. In this stagily-
effective frock, with her golden hair a-hanging
down her back, I thought Mrs. Brough (all
through “Fedora” one never forgot it was Mrs.
Brough) looked her very best. Miss Faber, as
Countess Olga, wore a most killing brown hat in
the last act, and was otherwise modishly arrayed,
but some of the Countess’s he-friends ought to
have taken the precaution of mangling their
obviously-borrowed dress-clothes before coming
on. One youth especially was an ocean of
wrinkles—he really looked as if father’s pants did
not fit Johnny. Otherwise the “ Fedora ” staging
left nothing to be desired —except that Cecil Ward
had painted his face so red that he looked like a
candidate for Echo Farm.
Gov. and Lady Lamington,
potentates, are the most juvenile V.R. couple in
Australia, and quite a romantic interest attaches
to them, as they haven’t been married a year yet.
His Ex. is said to be tall and slight and very
young, but he doesn’t seem too young to know the
hanky-panky of the governing business. His
sympathetic visit to the poor people crushed under
an awning while waiting to see him pass was
worthy of ex-Govemor Carington. On dit that
Lady Lamington’s millinery is astonishing
Northern society, which usually dresses itself as if
it had every intention of ultimately paying the bill.
An awkward contretemps occurred on Gov.
Hampden’s arrival at Bathurst for the Show last
week. The V.R. train was half-an-hour before its
time—which was a new experience for Lord
Hampden, at any rate, who is more accustomed to
being after than before his appointment. Gov.
Hampden’s engine-driver got him there too soon,
and the consequence was that H.E. and suite were
left hanging about the station like empty poultry
crates until somebody alarmed a local potentate or
two. I hear that the Mayor has been apologising
ever since.
Gov. Hampden’s small son is said to be a paragon
among little boys, and a pattern to every urchin
in the land. He was allowed to go one Sunday
for a spin with a Sydney yachtsman, and when
the craft came to anchor the hos i j, on
there was too much gold-top and gold •
board one or two neighboring sailing-boats tor a
youth to be allowed to comprehen » he found
V.R. cherub below. Coming down later, hefound
him dutifully reading a prayer-book. If that boy
doesn’t look out, he will find himself the heio ot a
Sunday-school tract.
On Saturday ending Easter week, a Sydney
daily paper commented on the fact that neither
muslcf painting, nor the drama had been honored
at any of the Easter Government-House festnMms.
No doubt it was merely a coincidence that on
Monday invitations to the Governor s ball on the
following evening were marked urgent, ana
issued to members of the precessions, many o
whom attended the “ hop ” and partook ot Mis
Excellency’s hospitality. A much-bediamonded
gentleman from Poland voiced the feeling of the
assembly when he breathed in an audible whisper,
“ Vot a fine program ve could fix up if everybody
gave a turn. ”
Ed. Pinaud’s “Sapho” brand of perfumery is
the most successful superfluity of the age, so I was
pleased to receive from M. Moreau, the sole agent
in Australia, a delicious assortment of reviving
perfumes, “ sels Sapho ” warranted to cure Show-
ground and Art Gallery headaches in one sniff,
and smooth “Sapho” soap, all artistically done
up in floral caskets. The handsome way these
perfumes are arranged makes them an ornamental
adjunct to the- best regulated toilet-tables and
their fragrance .is flower-like.
One of two ladies, who had availed themselves
of the opportunity of inspecting the Sunbeam, was
heard to remark, when, leaving, “ What a wonder
Lord Brassey hasn’t a .billiard-table on board !
University Commemoration day without the
students seems something like limelight without
Rignold ; but it really was time the Senate put its
foot on wit and humor which seldom passed the
primitive stage of farmyard noises.
Miss Alice Muskett, the clever young Sydney
artist now sojourning in the Latin quarter of
Paris, has just had a picture accepted at the Salon.
Considering the sheet-time Miss Muskett has been
away from her native heath —she is Victorian-
born, by the way —this success speaks volumes for
the preliminary training she received in Aus-
tralia.
At Poverty Point.
Marie Elster scores a point in England by informing her
interviewers that she succeeded Frances Saville as Mar-
jorie at Melb. Princess’s. Frances Saville has made a big
name, and Elster is evidently coming on fast.
The first night of “Fbdora” at Sydney Criterion was
graced by the presence of Premier Reid, who flirted most
audaciously with his wife in a box.
The English Lord Chamberlain, in refusing to license
“ Joseph of Canaan,” was probably thinking of Mrs.
Potiphar.
Haresfoot : “ Williamson would always be a high-class
actor, if not great, his only lack being that purely natural
soul pathos, which marks Jefferson’s Caleb Plummer, and
is absent from Toole’s. Williamson’s Stofel deteriorated
through the necessity of coming down to the audience,
which weighs with every player. His Rip Van Winkle
was singularly creditable as an original study, while other
actors were under Jefferson’s thrall. In Kerry he was
quite perfect.”
Dampier’s first three nights in Bendigo (Vic.) averaged
£l5B a night. Record provincial business !
Pro. : Your sketch of J. C. Williamson omits to
mention the actor-manager’s “first appearance” on the
stage. It was in an Indian drama, “The Jibbernanisy,”
a thorough theatrical “ dime novel.” Therein young
Williamson played Little Tom Bruce, a “ one line ” part.
This “ first appearance ” was without his guardian’s con-
sent, and to prevent recognition J.C. disguised himself in
a gigantic muffler and great coat. When Tommy’s cue
arrived, from behind the muffler came a growling, unintelli-
gible gurgle. Not a word of the speech was decipherable,
and the youngster “got off” flurried, hot and uncom-
fortable, conscious only that he had made a failure.
J.C. at this time was only twelve years of age.”
By a curious coincidence the B. and B. Co. produced
‘ ‘ The Importance of Being Earnest ” at Sydney Cri. on
the anniversary of O. Wilde’s arrest.
A wave of prosperity has set in for “ the Firm.” “ Djin-
Djin” at Sydney Lyceum is repeating the Melb. success;
“Trilby” at Melb. Princess’s is running the record-
breaking “ Djin-Djin ” a close race ; and the Comic Opera
Co. at Adelaide is drawing the largest houses known for
years.
Mrs. Brough, sen. (Miss Romer) is at present in England,
where she has been, or is to be, operated upon for cata-
ract. The Bulletin is sorry to hear of her troubles.
The young-looking George Trader, of Trilby Co., takes
strong exception to the assertion that his name is “ un-
mistakably Yankee.” He was born in the North of Eng-
land, and says he has never met anyone in the States of
the same name.
A Bulletin correspondent George Trader, whose
lively Zou Zou is a strong feature of Trilby, is Boucicault’s
double in size and manner, though his touch is a little
lighter. He has played most of Boucicault’s parts in
’Murka, and as the Melb. public have fallen in love with
him as one woman Brough should make a bid for his ser-
vices when partner Dot departs hence. Trader also re-
sembles Boucy in a deep veneration for Pinero.
The’Murkan mummers say that the Melb. Princess’s
production of “Trilby” excels any seen in their country
in respect of the third act setting, the dancing in the
studio, and the general stage management.
About 7000 copies of “ Trilby ” have already been sold
from Cole’s Book Arcade, Melbourne. W. and M. by ar-
rangement with Cole, have their finger on the pulse of the
“ Trilby ” boom. Each copy of the book is furnished with
a neat advt. “ dodger ” supplied by the Firm, and at short
intervals an application for another 1000 “dodgers” is
telephoned from the Arcade to the Princess’s The book
pushes the show, and the show sells the book, so when
treas. Tallis meets Cole in the street they exchange smiles
like the augurs of old. s
Note the flaw in Godfrey’s clever make-up at Melb.
Tivoli : a snow-white head and a beet-red face under-
neath—blanc-mange surrounded by raspberry-jam.
Melba is returning to Eng. from the States with shekels
galore, and is to appear in June, at Covent Garden, at a
very high salary. There is no word now of her Australian
trip. You must wait for that, remarks The Bulletin’s
London correspondent, until her voice is wrecked, of
which there is no present appearance.
Why didn’t the Firm have the “Trilby” posters pro-

tion of Du Mauner s particular art; and, if well done
more effective than the eternal colored litho.

One of the most popular “ turns ” at a N.Y. dime
museum is that of a Frenchman who eats raw cats for
the amewsment of the multitude.
A performance of “Dandy Dick,” announced by a
suburban touring co. (professional), was stopped by B.
and 8., the other day, by threats of legal proceedings.
“Professor” Richard, the wild-haired “healer,” has
been having another awful exposure from the Calcutta
press. Richard has now been exposed almost all over the
civilised world, and he booms along serenely and makes
money just the same.
Polly Emery, despite her farewell benefit and the many
affecting paragraphs in the papers, isn’t going to England
just yet" She stays to play Audrey in “As You Like It,”
during the Potter-Bellew season, at Sydney Lyceum.
Bellew used to complain of some one who personated
him. Was it Reuben Keirl ?
Lalla Miranda’s voice is doing wonders under Rente
Richards’s tuition. Le Gil Blas, L’Echo de Paris,
L’Evenement, and other Paris papers praise her unstint-
edly. “ A delicious, exquisite, marvellous-voiced woman
with a brilliant future ” is amongst the encomiums passed
upon her.
Maggie Moore’s co. was recently stranded at Cobar
(N.S.VV.) for want of coach accommodation to take it to
Broken Hill. So the co. lighted out by rail for Mel-
bourne, thence overland to Adelaide, and from Adelaide
by train again to the Barrier township, and arrived up to
time after 1600 miles railway travelling, and sleeping in
the train most of the time. The way of the mummer in
the back-blocks is pretty hard.
Melba’s fear is that she will wax fat and matronly. Her
panacea is three cold baths a day.

are requested to wipe their Trilbies.” So the English
language is manufactured.

Our excellent Gaiety friends, Leedham Bantock, Fritz
Rimma and Mrs. Phelps, have found engagements in a

Dear Bulletin, —You allow someone to support “Dot”
Boucicault’s ridiculous explanation of the reason why we
have no great Australian actors (the prevalence of the
Australian accent) with an assertion that Australians do
not speak English, backed up by the yarn about “ plites,”
and “ kike,” and “ gripes.” Now, that yarn is a chestnut,
and it is an English chestnut, and was first told to the
discredit of the English-speaking peoples at the fountain
head, where the flow should be pure and undefiled.
Please, who do speak English if not the Australians ? Do
the English of Cornwall, Lancashire, Cumberland, or
Middlesex ? Is the worst Australian accent discoverable
by the nice ear of an expert like Mr. Boucicault nearly as
far from perfection as any one of the few-score English
dialects, or is the Australian’s grammar as formless and
void as that of the average Englander? And yet England
has produced a few great actors, if not Mr. Boucicault,
who will probably claim that he speaks the purest Dublin.
—S.S.
A gushing N. York critic on Yvette Guilberte “ She
has always been a striver, has this frail-built young
Frenchwoman, with the slenderness of Diana and the
earnestness of Fanny Davenport. And, if passing sorrows
have not furrowed the nacre of her white forehead, if
tears have not reddened her blonde eyes, it was because
her forehead was of pure Paros, and because tears under
her golden lashes turned to a limpid rosewater that pre-
served the little blue flower of poesy which struggled to
live in the large pale eyeballs.”
The Trilby foot, the Ellen Terry skip, and the Bern-
hardt mouth have dropped out of fashion with ’Murkan
women, and the latest craze is the Duse Eyelid Droop,
which consists in imitating the Italian actress’s drop ol
the eyelid in order to obtain “an expression of dreamy
and deeply-lustrous softness.”
Who will do up “ Trilby ”as a grand opera? Mascagni
should be put on the job.
The sexes, it seems, are still unequal in
South Australia. A letter published in Adelaide
Register :
Sir, —It is to be hoped that at the coming- elections a
little more courtesy will be shown to the ladies than was
accorded to them at the Town Hall the night of the
Premier’s address to his constituents. Five ladies stood
in the body of the hall for nearly two hours before it
occurred to one of the many hundreds of men to offer
them a seat, and then the offered chair was one just
vacated by a man who was leaving- the room. Need I say
that, as he took so long to find out that the lady might
like a seat, it was courteously declined ? If this is what
we are to expect on polling-day, I fear some of us will stay
at home.— Etiquette.
This Saturday (April 25) S.A. Assembly
general elections will begin and end. The present
contest has excited enormous interest locally,
inasmuch as it resolves itself into a pitched battle
between the National Ass and democratic parties.
Owing to its splendid organisation, the Labor-
party carried all before it in ’93, but since then
the Ass has organised, and, adopting- “manipula-
tion of the rolls ” as the chief item in its policy,
has greatly altered the condition of things. It is
not unlikely the Tories will win back one or two
metropolitan seats after all, Labor members
M‘Pherson, Wood and Hourigan not being
at all certain of re-election. But the Ass is in
fear as to the country constituencies. Many pro-
minent Tory members of last Assembly, notably
John Downer (Opposition leader), Howe, Caldwell,
Riddoch and Glynn, are in danger of succumbing
to local Liberal candidates who have adopted the
Ministerial programme, which, owing to its popu-
larity with the producers, has much enhanced
democratic chances in the provinces. But every-
thing depends upon that mysterious element—the
great woman vote. The Ass party professes to
believe it will go almost en bloc to Conservative
candidates ; the Democrats hope that at worst it
will be evenly divided.

The boat may carry three—hooray 1—
And he will carry one !

The Sydney unemployed have asked Col.
Sec. Brunker for shelter and blankets. Poor
whitepfeller ! The decaying Australian nigger
gets his annual blanket as a matter of course.
With Queensland represented on the Fede-
ral Convention, Reidites reckon Sydney’s chances
of being made the capital of the Federation as 10
to 3; without Queensland, the chances are reckoned
3 to 10.
The rejection of Attorney-
General Byrnes by North Bris-
bane is said to have been in
part brought about by the
vigorous circulation of the
opinion that his return by the
premier constituency would
give him a solid title to the
reversion of the Premiership.
Byrnes being an R.C., the old
Adam of bigotry accordingly
put in his work. This is quite
likely. Queensland has not, so
far, had an R.C. Premier. It
has had them of most other re-
ligions, but no R.C. has entered
in ; and when John Douglas,
then Premier, allowed Bishop
Quinn to marry him, it was
seen by his friends, ;..iu the
result verified the forecast that,
although he stoutly declared he
was still a Protestant, his
ruling days were over.,
“ Black-labor Callan,” re-
marks a Bulletin correspon-
dent, “has again romped in
for Mount Morgan. This time
he had a majority of 306 ; last
time only 43. It is hard to be-
lieve that this is a true indica-
tion of the state of political
feeling on the Mount. The
contest was purely between
Labor and Capital (as personi-
fied by the Mt. Morgan Co.)
and not a political struggle at
all. One of the sights of the
day was the gangs of workmen
being marched down from the
works to the polling in
the charge of petty bosses. The
election afforded excellent op-
portunities of ‘crawling,’ and
these were not thrown away.
Labor also accounts for its
great defeat by the old cry of
R.C. influence. Anyhow, Fat-
man has got home again.
Political professions and
the legal profession don’t keep
good time together. The legal
M.P. who is a democrat on the
platform has often to be a ram-
pant Tory in practice. Witness
Deakin, 'for the ship-owners,
before the Sangster Shippmg-
board. It will be well for Alfred,
M.P., xf his radical supporters
out Essendon way have not fol-
lowed too closely the doings and
sayings of Alfred, lat Mans
legal adviser, before the Board.
The common elector does not
appreciate the subtle distinction
between the lawyer and the
politician. Deakin should care-
fully educate his constituents
up to the fact that his utter-
ances as a lawyer are always
“ without prejudice.”

The Jap.-trotted N.S. Wales Attorney-
General, J. Want, tells how the Chinese in the in-
terior of China, who have heard at all about the
China-Jap. war, believe that the invaders were
beaten back into the Yellow Sea, since they didn’t
march on to Pekin. Australia’s hope is that
China may sleep on thus soundly for another
century.
A hundred and ten Australian natives, led
by three aboriginals, are alleged to have marched
solidly up to vote for Byrnes at the Warwick
polling-booths. The writer has always had a
sneaking sort of satisfaction in being an Australian
native—an irrational, sentimental kind of satis-
faction, quite unconnected with logic—but it’s
fast leaving him.

The other day a N.S.W. firm secured a
Government contract for 1000 casks cement, and
the shoddy organs, especially Sydney D.T., cheered
vociferously, and said that this proved loca
industries can get along all right under Iree-
trade. As a matter of fact, the duty is still on
cement, and will remain on till Ist July, so the
case only shows that local industries can get along
all right under Protection. The cement industry
is simply in process of flickering out because buyers
are holding back till the duty comes off two months
hence. But, considering that it is still on, the
whoop about cement being a flourishing 1 reetrade
industry is interesting.
N.S.W. Lands Minister Carruthers, in his
speech at Hay, bound himself down to be member
of no Government that will raise the land-tax
above Id. Reid vowed the same thing some
months ago. Thus a Conservative ring is being
gradually formed in the land-tax camp, and, as
usual, two lawyers are the first to establish their
claim to lead the new party.
Some rough drafts of the proposed Bill for
throwing the support of indigent inmates in N.S.
Wales Govt, institutions on the shoulders of
well-to-do relatives, which there is hope of sub-
mitting to Parliament next session, indicate that
not only the well-off children, but the well-off
grandchildren of indigents will be compulsorily
distrained.
One-half million sterling represents the
amount of money involved in the N.S.W. sugar-
duty between now and the date on which Premier
Reid proposes to finally extinguish the tax. In
other words, Reid gives the sugar interest £500,000
more than he gives other manufacturing or agri-
cultural interests—and this is the point which no
eloquence can explain away.
Now that a new Commissioner of Vic.
Railways is appointed, revelations as to the run-
down condition of rolling-stock and railroads are
looming in the near distance. The first twelve-
months of John Mathieson’s reign may be ex-
pected to produce a big advance on the previous
year’s deficit, accounted for by a vast accumulation
of necessary repairing jobs and an unavoidable
expenditure on new goods.
If the N.S.W. (or any other) Government
adopted the sensible plan of creating a State Drink
Department, and ran all the groggeries, the adul-
teration of snakejuice would be killed in one act,
and comparative sobriety would reign as a sequence
to the fact that the Liquor Officer would take care
not to jeopardise his billet by over-supplying the
buyer. But these very reforms would extinguish
the “shocking example,” and the prohibitionist’s
occupation would be gone. Which is probably the
plumb reason why the prohibitionist glares side-
ways at the “ Gothenberg ” or other State-drink-
selling proposals, and vows that his conscience—
his conscience ! —will not permit him to coquet
with sin.
Considering the bitter wail of the Out-
landers in the Transvaal because the Boers don’t
allow them the franchise, it would be interesting
to know how many of these Outlanders were high-
dried Tories when in their own country. The
genuine Tory is a man who has the suffrage him-
self, and snorts furiously at the idea of giving it
to another man ; being the man on top, he pro-
fesses himself utterly unable to understand what
the man underneath can want with a voice in the
management of affairs. And yet when he goes to
a strange country, and there finds himself the man
underneath, and the man who is on top snorts at
the very idea of a thing like him wanting to have
a voice in anything, his howl of rage and astonish-
ment can be heard over four continents.
Q. Labor-party is trying with some in-
genuity to get Macdonald - Paterson and the
Telegraph crowd to form a new Ministerial party
vice the Nelson-cum -Courier crowd, and the
Worker promises qualified Labor support. The
idea is to sell support for concessions after the
fashion of the N.S.W. Labor-party. So far,
Paterson hasn’t given any overt signs of a wish
to wear uneasy shoes like Reid’s.
S.A. Tories loudly claim that the local
women owe the suffrage to them, and that J. L.
Stirling, an Ass man in the Council, was the first
to advocate the principle. What Stirling pro-
posed was to enfranchise women with property, in
all about 5000. What Kingston actually did was
to enfranchise 55,000 women on the basis of their
womanhood alone, and he carried the measure
through despite violent Tory opposition.

‘‘ I have a paper on Federation in my
pocket,” said Premier Braddon to a Tas. meeting
the other night, “ but I haven’t time to read it. I
will publish it in an English magazine and you
can read it there ”! !! Even an Australian opinion
on Australian Federation has, it seems, to be
exported to England and imported back again
before the local mind can tackle it! The Bulle-
tin hopes Premier Braddon’s next meeting will
tell him it hasn’t time to hear him talk ; but if he
likes he can go to England and speak, and his
speech can be cabled out.
The Hawthorn (Melb.) Liberal Association
has the honor to be first in the field with an Anti-
Fat' Man war-cry. Last week the H.L.A. con-
vened a conference to discuss the best means of
wiping out Softgoods Sargood when he offers him-
self for re-election to Vic. Leg. Council in Sept,
next. Thus far the real feeling of property-voters,
as a whole, towards Fat Man’s calm monopoly of
the Council has never been tested. The average
householder didn’t go to the poll. Moreover, there
wasn’t a poll, as often as not. The retiring Tory
waddled back to his seat unopposed.
The Turner Govt, does not intend carrying
out the recommendations of the Lormer Board re
a reform of the Vic. honorary magistracy. The
Premier “ feels that it is unnecessary to take such
a drastic step as that of superseding the honorary
bench.” This means that, in a few years’ time,
things will be exactly as they were, ; nd the
“honorarium” J.P. who parades town with his
hand behind him, palm uppermost, will be as
glaring an abomination as he was a few months
since; also, the kept magistrate of the street-
walker will grace the bench as dutifully as of yore.
Can sailors be compelled to put to sea in
an unseaworthy ship ? That appears to be the
crux of the action brought against the captain of
the Nicaraguan barque Dominion, for wages due
at Port Melbourne police-court. There is no
doubt that in the case of British ships the sailor
would be safe in refusing to go, but it is argued
that the Merchant Shipping Act doesn’t apply to
“foreign” vessels. There is a provision in the
Act, however, which, it is argued, provides that
in case of conflict between the foreign and the
local law in such matters, the Merchant Ship-
ping Act is to prevail.
Under present political conditions in
N.S.W. it is the height of frog-swollen tomfoolery
for the O’Sullivan-under-Lyne party to canvass
for public-meeting resolutions that “ every dis-
trict should petition the Governor to dissolve the
present Leg. Assembly.” Were H.E. to take
upon himself any such impertinence of power, a
revolution to deport him forcibly would be justi-
fiable. Voters, whether fools or not, must abide
by the actions of the Parliament they elect, and
only the “ responsible advisers ” can force upon the
Governor a dissolution. A plebiscite to the
Governor ? Pah ! An appeal to a doorknob !
S.A. Register has, for weeks past, per-
sistently accused Premier Kingston of inconsis-
tency as a politician. Not long ago the now
ultra-Tory Register was itself quite democratic,
and, amongst other items, was a strong advocate
of direct land-values taxation and adult suffrage.
The circumstances under which the Register
parted with Medway Day and abandoned all its
democratic ideas are still fresh in the public
memory. The Register would, no doubt, have
remained Liberal had the Ass not threatened to
establish a third Adelaide daily paper. “Needs
must when the devil drives.”

THE ATTORNEY-GENERAL’S
BOOTS.
“ Mr. Want did not go to Japan solely for
the sake of his health, though, no doubt,
that was a first consideration. ... I
wanted a pair of boots made at Kobe, and
after a brisk search I discovered a Chinaman
who understood the business.”
Was that bootmaker a returned Chino-
Australian, and had he lived in Woolloo-
mooloo ? If so, Jack’s boots would look like
the above.

Personal Items
One of the new N.S.W. Js.P. is a bum-
bailiff.
David Syme, having got Speight off his
chest, again turns to office business.
A nephew of Chinese Gordon now “over-
sees ” one of the largest tracts of country west of
the Darling.
Mrs. Morris, wife of Prof. Morris of Melb.
University, and eldest daughter of the late Chief
Justice Higinbotham, has died of pneumonia.
Major Howie, Melbourne’s wealthiest ab-
sentee, pockets <£40,000 a year ground-rent from a
Collins-street block —on which he has never set
eyes.
The sudden appointment of Mathieson to
the Vic. railway general-managership was a
terrible knock-out to Speight, who shows signs of
feeling it keenly.
The Victorian Government paid the whole
expense of journalist Gilbert Smith’s return to
Westralia, whence they took him on a bogus
charge of perjury.
Reported that Judge Buckley, of Maori-
land, appointed only the other day, will resign his
judgeship owing to ill-health. The Bulletin is
curious to know whether Patrick will resign his
pension.
Pastor Bevan, who fired the first shot at
Melbourne magisterial corruption and then backed
down, leaving the fighting to Lormer, now mani-
fests a disposition to boast that it was he who set
the ball rolling.
Edith Trilby Crane, interviewed in Mel-
bourne, says her impression of that city is that it
is “a second Washington.” Yes, without the
“ Wash.” Let Miss Crane wait till she has smelt
a little more of it.
An old Sydney identity, Mr. Jacob Marks,
has died in London. Mr. Marks was formerly in
business in George-street, opposite to where the
Cafe Francais stands, and was one of the earliest
N.S.W. gold-buyers.
The late Alexander C. Habbe, who died of
cancer in Melbourne the other day, and his brother
Nicholas (a figure-painter who died in Sydney
some years ago) were alleged to be natural sons of
a late King of Denmark.
Trenwith, M.L. A., was not unduly honored
by the Melbourne Trades Hallites prior to his de-
parture for Europe. A little more fuss over the
strong man in spectacles wouldn t have been
superfluous, considering that he is the one marked
success among the Labor members.
Obit.—Arthur W. Hill, “Vigilant,” of
London Sportsman, originally an Adelaide law-
clerk and afterwards an Adelaide Advertiser
Collins, Photo., Sydney.
THE MANLY TRAGEDY.
Above are printed portraits of Miss Florence Collins, of North
Sydney, and her lover, Andrew Ernest Arkle, who on 16th inst. shot her
and himself at Manly. Miss Collins was 19 years of age ; Arkle a year
her senior. The pair had been on terms of intimacy for four years past,
and a short time ago he asked her to marry him, and was refused. Never-
theless, the intimacy continued, and frequent meetings appear to have
fanned to flame Arkle’s jealousy of a suspected rival. Miss Collins
accepted without foreboding his company to Manly on the fatal evening,
and an hour or two later she was found shot dead on a seat on the Manly
Esplanade. Arkle 'was alive when discovered, but died shortly afterwards.
reporter, more particularly remembered for his
eccentricities and his pug dog. A good pressman
and a thorough Bohemian. Age 49.
The Argus turns dog on Speight as it did
on Patterson, and lauds the newly-appointed Rail-
way-Commissioner Mathieson as the saviour of
the province. Speight’s opportunist “ friends ”
may not sympathise with him, but the worst of
his enemies extends him at least pity, for his
administrative vices were but virtues run to seed.

the Vic. family of that ilk, and an old-time

emigrated from Tasmania in ’39, taking up a big
swee P of country along the Barwon river, Geelong.

The Lascar sailor who stood up to the
, eeo nd mate of the Australian and pulled the cap-
tain overboard and had a square set-to with him
i n the water, recalls the old-time class of Aus
tralian coasting skippers, such as Jack M‘Lean,
“ Hell-fire Jack,” who made it his boast that his
threat was his bond. Of many yarns told of him,
Ibis is at least authentic. While crawling up the
winding Yarra, a swell of high degree climbed up
on the bridge. Off the bridge, damn you, sir,
sald J a °k. The swell merely pulled out his field-
glasses and said, Aw. if you re not off the
bridge when Ive counted twenty I’ll fling you
overboard.” The swell passenger was amused.
“Still here? Over you go.” And over he did go,
and was only saved from drowning by a fluke.

counsel carries round a
set of translations of
Heine and Goethe, done
when he was confined as
an alleged lunatic. These
he regards as conclusive
evidence of his sanity
during that period.
Which recalls Frank
Hutchinson, who, one
day, met a newspaper-
man who had long been
ill, and had just returned
from Europe. “ Glad to
see you back,” said Hut-
chinson, “ you seem all

I wrote those signed arti-

them see the contrary.”
“ Ah, poor fellow,” said
Hutchinson, tapping his
forehead solemnly, “ I
never had the slightest
doubt of your brain my-
self —until I saw those
articles. ”
