When Gov. Hampden tells N.S. Wales
that, “ if you can only get a combination of good
seasons and good prices, it will do you more good
than anything that can be done by man,” the ideas
in his “ kobbera ” must be fairly butting each
other to escape from the fire that is raging in his brain.
The new strike at Hudson Brothers’ big
engineering works, near Sydney, is caused by the
firm’s persistent anxiety to feed a horse on one
straw a day. The men originally earned an
average of about <£2 10s. a week, and consented to
a reduction to about <£2, and then to a further
reduction to about 30s. The question now is
whether they will rub along on about 255. and a
share in what are facetiously called “profits,”
which share wasn’t offered until there were no
profits and little prospect of any. They—or some
of them —say they won’t; but as the horse has
been trained down so fine now, there’s really
no reason why he shouldn’t live on air, which is
unquestionably very filling at the price.
Mortimer Franklyn, the cheeky person
who cost those sterling gentlemen, the Melbourne
Hentys, thousands over his wild ventures, the
Federal Australian and World, has of late
made thousands a year in London as an insurance-
canvasser ; but he is again bankrupt —all along of
Stock Exchange gambling. In explanation of his
Melb. failure he told the London registrar that he
“had endeavored to introduce high-class news-
papers, but they were over the heads of the people.”
The impudent Franklyn was a great man in Mel-
bourne when the Hentys were spoon-feeding him
with thousands, and he always had a footman
standing outside his office-door with folded arms.
His papers, though occasionally showing good
points, were in many respects beneath contempt-
nothing ever printed in Australia matched the
Federal Australian for “ Society ” crawlsome-
ness. The enthusiastic worminess of its style
gave color to the gibe that it was conducted by a
barber.
The Canadian House of Commons has just
given up the ghost, after completing two re-
cord sittings of 129 hours and 80 hours on the
Manitoba Schools Bill, during which the Bible
and the Bab Ballads were read at length, and the
local Neilds wagged jawbones with unprecedented
fury. The trouble (as South Australians may
note) is the old trouble of sectarian education,
from which “loyal ” Canada has hardly begun to
free herself. Nowhere do Orange and Green rave
more savagely than in Canada, the paradise of
Protestant arid Catholic. bigots alike. In the
eastern provinces denominational education still
flourishes as a matter of course. The western pro-
vince of Manitoba, six years ago, made an effort to
establish unsectarian State schools, lopped off the
R.C. endowment, was hauled before the Dominion
Parliament and the British Privy Council for
“ unconstitutional action,” and has been fighting
the question ever since. The present Bill, on
which Canada is asked to give an opinion
at a general election in June, is an effort
to compel Manitoba to reinstate the R.C.
schools, but exactly how the compulsion is to be
applied is a problem. The Dominion Ministry is
roasted between two fires, and all the old passions
of Bartholomew’s Day, and the Boyne water, and
the “ glorious and immortal memory,” are let
loose Religion and Rum are the two best stimu-
lants humanity knows of, but when Religion
starts in earnest Rum is nowhere.
Dicky Smith, the familiar little fur-capped
Crown Law messenger of Melb., passed from this
sphere last week. Dicky lent a bit of money to
bosses, though, to be sure, he was not such a
dabster in that line as some of the constables, who
have despised the bills of a saymor and gone for
the inspectors and shupenntendents with bull-
in-china-shop effect, if the occasion arose.

The portrait herewith represents Alex.
Christian Habbe, the once well-known scenic artist.,
Habbe was a Dane by birth, and was in his day and
in his own particular line a great man, but greater
men came along and he faded into a species of
eclipse. The best thing he did was the drop-
scene of the old Victoria Theatre, Sydney, and he
did it over again for Melbourne Opera House.
He was at one time in partnership with Wilson,
the well-known theatrical manager, who survives
both him and his later associate, Majeroni. Habbe
lived in retirement in Melbourne for a long time
before his death, and this generation hardly re-
membered even his name, until his obituary notice
recalled him vaguely to memory.
The Late A. C. HABBE, Scenic Artist.
Advt. from S.M. Herald of April 11 :
QONSCIENCE.
When I was a (steerage) passenger during the August
trip of theU.S.S. Rotokino from Auckland to Sydney, I
meanly stole half-a-crown from an article of clothing be-
longing to Mr. Roberts, and two boxes of matches from a
coat belonging to another (name unknown), both steerage
passengers.
The pipe, with case, that I raffled on board, and the
other articles that I sold to members of the crew, I did
under false pretences, for these all had been stolen by me
in New Zealand.
A week or so later, at Eden, when on board the U.S.S.
Oonah, on the way to Hobart, I swindled a person who
had come off to the steamer by giving him a foreign
copper coin instead of—and not so large as —a penny, as
part payment for a stamp for a letter for New Zealand,
that he had kindly consented to stamp and post for me oh
his return to shore.
Shortly after my return from Hobart I stole a piece of
Tobacco and a Pocket Knife that I had found on a seat in
the Botanical Gardens. I used the knife for a time, and
some weeks later I left both articles at the Superintendent
of the Garden’s house for the owner should he apply for
them.
I hid from several who had taken tickets in the raffle
that there were more members for it when it came off than
I had at first represented there would be. I was also
unaware of the value of the pipe and case, and also of
nearly all the articles I sold, and yet I stated positively
that such and such had been the price. In some instances
I asked for and obtained more than I knew was the value
of an article.
I hereby beg the pardon of ALL.
ROBERT A. H. FRASER. February 2, 1896. Address :
General Post Office, Adelaide, S.A.

THIS Cbmpay is being formed to acquire Gold Mining 1 Leases Nos. 1782 and 2133, situate about twelve miles north of
White Feather and well-known as the “General Gordon.”
r* The property consists of 41J acres on the north shore of Red Lake, which at all times yields at a slight depth more
than sufficient water for milling purposes. At the present time there is a large volume of water on the surface of the
Lake. Since its discovery the prospectors have sunk one shaft 70ft. and one shaft 30ft., and opened up several large
costeens and trenches, and this work has confirmed the reputation of the mine for phenomenal richness, which was
indicated at the surface. Special attention is drawn to the published reports of Mr. G. R. Fearby, Dr. Chewings, J.
Renowden, and N. W. Harper, J.P., Manager of the Robinson Gold Mines, White Feather (Kanowna), all of whom are
unanimous in their opinion of the great value of this property which the Directors confidently believe to be the richest
in Western Australia.
It is the intention of the Directors to immediately proceed with the erection of a ten stamp mill, and at the same
time vigorously develop the mine. They also intended having a trial crushing before the allotment took place, but
owing to the late heavy rains rendering the transit of ore to the White Feather battery difficult this was found to be
impracticable.
The present issue of Shares will place the Company in possession of more than £lO,OOO working capital, which it
is believed will be ample, as there is an enormous quantity of ore ready for milling which will yield a very high
average. It is proposed to develope the mine on the open cut system, the position of the lodes being admirably
adapted for this method of working.
Steps are now being taken for the establishment of a London register.
The price to be paid for the property is £17,000 in cash and 40,000 fully paid-up Shares.
Applications for Shares shotild be made on the form enclosed to the Brokers of the Company. If the Shares
allotted be less than the number applied for, the surplus of the amount paid on deposit will be applied in reducing the
amount due on allotment, and where no allotment is made the deposit will be returned in full. Prospectuses and
application forms may be obtained at the offices of the Company, and also of the Brokers. It is intended to allot the
Shares .pro rata.
- SUMMARY OF REPORTS ON THE GENERAL GORDON.
From a carefully-selected bulk sample of the whole of the lode matter as exposed in the bottom of the shaft, I
obtained"the«splendid result of gold, 4oz. 19dwt. sgr. per ton 22401 b. In selecting this sample I carefully avoided and
threw out all ore that I had any suspicion would assay at all high, and this result may be accepted as the value of the
ordinary ore.
From stone that showed just a few colors the somewhat startling result of 8490 z. 12dwt. lgr. was obtained. The
ore shows gold freely enough, and when broken again and again it is seen to be well distributed through it. Near the
top of the shaft on the southern end good gold may be seen in the stone. At intervals this chute may be traced down
the wall to within 2ft. of the bottom of the shaft. There the gold becomes more plentiful and forms a yellow speckled
band that appears to pitch steep to the south. The ore hereabouts is very rich. I had blocks of this ore removed,
and the band when I left stood still in the face. I can only add that, in my opinion, immense bodies of highly payable
ore will be opened up in this mine, much of which as we have seen wil be phenomonally rich in gold.—’l remain,
respectfully yours,
General Gordon, Coolgardie, Oct. 15, 1895. OHAS. CHEWINGS, Geologist, Ph.D., F.G.S., etc.
So far as the developments are concerned the results are highly satisfactory, and the probabilities of the property
turning out an immense success are very great so far as the stone in the shaft is concerned. It is enormously rich,
and I see no reason why this same phenomenal class of stone should not exist elsewhere in the formation, as there is
nothing on the surface at the shaft to indicate rich stone, and there is no gainsaying the fact but that the property
has an excellent prospect in view.—Yours faithfully,
Kanowna, 16th October, 1895. N. W. HARPER, Manager Mt. Robinson G.M. Co.
The rock taken out of this shaft is simply impregnated with gold, the main golden chute dips to the south, and
during the time of my visit Dr. Chewings broke down some of the finest samples of gold bearing stone I have ever
seen. I myself broke down with a hammer and gad several pieces from right across the S. face, every piece of which
showed gold. The golden stone given you by' the prospectors for assay and show specimens cannot came near in point
of richness that broken out by Dr. Chewings, and which I should estimate to go several hundred ounces per ton. There
is every possibility and probability of this rich gold going down, as it is not in rich dabs and patches, but evenly dis
tributed through the stone. —I remain, etc.,
Coolgardie, 15/10/95. JOHN O. RENOWDEN, Manager Bayley’s No. 2 South.

HOT CLIMATES.—“ I cannot refrain from
adding my testimony to the great efficiency of
your Fruit Salt’ here. I have a store, and as
numerous travellers are constantly passing and
the climate being extremely hot, frequently 110°
in the shade, there is naturally much drink con-
sumed. It is a colonial custom that if a traveller
drops in your store, to ask the proprietor to liquor •
I did ‘liquor’ for some considerable time and found
my liver increasing in consequence. Having been
recommended your excellent ‘ Fruit Salt ’ I
steadily applied myself to six bottles in accordance
with the instructions given, and I feel myself
bound to say that the result was not only satis-
factory, but, I believe, has permanently reduced
my liver to its normal size. Since then I invari-
ably use your ‘Fruit Salt’ when asked to ‘liquor’
in place of the beverage which inebriates and
doesn’t always cheer, and charge the customer
as per usual.—l remain, Sir, yours truly, W.
Schmidt, Barkly Rout, Griqualand West, South
Africa. J. C. Eno, Esq.” Sold by all chemists
and stores.

Some phenomenal “ tipping ” was accom-
plished over the late A.J.C. and Onkaparinga
meetings by Mr. A. W. Steel, of 133 Victoria-
parade, Fitzroy, Melbourne. Mr. Steel only ad-
vised over three races at each meeting—St. Leger,
Wallace ; Doncaster Handicap, Courallie ; Sydney
Cup, Wallace ; Onkaparinga Hurdles, Geometry ;
Steeple, Dungan; Cup, Preston. A sequence of
five wins, with Preston a good second. This per-
formance is the more meritorious, as Mr. S. only
sent the one horse for each race, and those weeks
before the races. Mr. S. is always good over
Sydney and Adelaide races, but this time he has
fairly eclipsed himself.*

Mark Twain Again.
There are few prominent Americans more favour-
ably known in Australia than “Mark Twain,” the
humorist. He was once. credited with a truism
which fairly illustrates the keenness of perception
and soundness of thought which gleam brightly
through and characterise his quaint diction. He
is reported as saying : —‘ ‘ There is no use filling
the upper story of a youth with scientific know-
ledge unless he has a fair stock of common-sense on
the ground floor.” Good, old-fashioned, common-
sense was what Mr. Hugh Barnett, a retired
Government servant, of Roma, Queensland, exer-
cised when he took advantage of his friends’ and
neighbours’ experience and chose a remedy for
indigestion and liver troubles which had cured
them. His experience justified that decision, and
is worthy of thoughtful consideration. He writes:
—“ From personal experience with Warner’s Safe
Cure I have no hesitation in advising persons
suffering from liver disease to take it in preference
to all other remedies. For a long time I endured
terrible suffering from that complaint. The pains
under the shoulder blades, down the back and in
my chest and side were so severe that I could get
no rest day or night. My appetite failed, and I
became so weak and worn out that I was obliged
to give up work of every kind. After spending
much time and money in other directions, I
started to take Warner’s Safe Cure, and after
using several bottles I am thankful to say that I
have quite recovered my former good health.”
Yic. School Paper for March, by autho-
rity of the Education Department, tells Vio.
youngsters all about Queensland “alligators,” joy-
fully oblivious of the fact that all the Aust.
saurians are “crocodiles.” The alligator is tho-
rough American, and that only.

“ Tattevsall” (George Adams) has pleasure in notifying his
numerous subscribers and patrons that he has had placed in his hands a
number of valuable SYDNEY CITY and SUBURBAN PROPERTIES,
which he intends distributing by BALLOT on TUESDAY, 30th JUNE,
1896.
The PROPERTIES comprise Land qnd Buildings in some of the best
portions of the city, and “ Tattersall ” can recommend this distribution
to all his customers, as the whole of the properties have been approved of
by him.
The FIRST PRIZE is of the value of £28,5.00, and consists of
splendid rent-producing properties in the heart of the city, which are
returning now a nett yearly rental of £866, though the rentals have been
greatly reduced to meet the existing depression.
THE SEVERAL PROPERTIES have been placed in distribution
at less than cost price to the owners, and there is little doubt that the
increased demand for Sydney City properties, which is already apparent,
will enable the fortunate prize winners, at an early date, to dispose of their
prizes for an amount equal to the value placed upon the properties.
PRIZE WINNERS will receive Title Deeds free of any deduction
whatever, including cost of conveyance and stamp duty, and LAND TAX
will be paid on properties for the year 1896.
WINNERS of £2O prizes have the option of taking the value in cash,
less 10 per cent.

VT OTIOE is hereby given that it is the intention of
BENJAMIN CRISPIN SIMPSON, of Sydney, in the
colony of New South Wales, Member of the Institute of
Civil Engineers, Esquire, to apply to the Parliament of
New South Wales during the next session for a Bill to
authorise the said BENJAMIN CRISPIN SIMPSON, or
his Assigns, to construct a Bridge and Approaches thereto
between the South and North Shores of Port Jackson, the
said Bridge and Approaches to commence at a point in
Cumberland-street, in the city of Sydney, about thirty-
three chains fifty links north from the intersection of
Cumberland-street with Charlotte-place, in the city of
Sydney, to Dawes Point, thence to and crossing the Waters
of Port Jackson to Milson’s Point, North Sydney, thence
to and terminating at a point in Alfred-street, about ten
chains fifty links north of the intersection of Dind-street
and the said Alfred-street, in the borough of North
Sydney ; to authorise the building over and diversion of
streets or roads; to authorise the appropriation or use of
certain lands required for or in connection with the carry-
ing out of the said work ; to make provision for compen-
sating the owners of and others interested in such lands;
to authorise the imposition of tolls on persons using the
said bridge ; to confer powers for the making and enforce-
ment of bylaws ; and for all other purposes which may be
incidental thereto. Dated at Sydney this 13th day of
April, 1896.
LEONARD GIBSON, 92 Pitt-street, Sydney, Solicitor
or the Bill.

The Royal Bank of Australia
LIMITED.
Head Office : MELBOURNE, VICTORIA.

SIXTEENTH REPORT.
Presented to the Shareholders at the Ordinary General Meeting on 29th April , 1896.
HDHE Directors beg to submit herewith balance-sheet and statement of accounts to 31st
Mai'cli, duly audited and certified, showing a profit for the half-year of £2099 Bs. 2d.,
which, with the balance previously at credit of profit and loss account, they propose to
carry forward. It is a matter for much regret that another session of Parliament has ended
without the passing of an amendment of the Companies Act, and that the Directors are
therefore still without the necessary legislative authority to write down capital or declare a
dividend out of the profits earned during the half-year.
The volume of business is well kept up, and it will be noticed that the bank’s financial
position continues strong, the cash reserves being 35 per cent, of the liabilities to the
public.
By order of the Board.
H. T. WILSON, Manager.

Judging by certain cycling ' sights to be
seen in the gloaming or at early morn in more or
less out-of-the-way spots, dressmakers have of late
been making some terrible bloomers.
One of the pleasantest, coolest, most
central and best-appointed restaurants in Sydney
is the Manhattan Dining Room, situated in the
basement of the great Equitable Building in
George-street, Sydney. It is in the middle of the
busiest portion of Sydney, and is in every respect
a high-class establishment with good cooking, good
attendance, a lavish bill of fare, and luxurious
fittings and furnishings.*
The latest controversy is re the fairness of the
“ push stroke ” at billiards, upon which opinion is much
divided. Mitchell has challenged Roberts to play him at
the odds of a third of the game, “ push stroke barred,
and John has replied accepting it, but the champion
sarcastically says he would rather present Mitchell with a
substantial cheque if he wins than allow him or his
friends to risk their money in backing him (Mitchell).

sit up.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Randwick victories of Wallace will, no
doubt, resurrect the familiar paper talk about Car-
bine’s prospects at ’Ome. There’s a lot of luck in breed-
ing. Judicious nicking is a potent factor. Trenton may
beat him hollow, and there’s no visible bar to Carnage
putting both in the background. Ragimunde and Son-of-
a-gun are the only two notable gets of Petronel, so far.
Petronel himself unexpectedly won the Two Thousand
once, retiring with 11 races to his credit—mostly Queen’s
Plates. He proved very mediocre in handicap society.
» * * «
Wallace, 100 to 5, is already the big Cup
favorite. Seeing that he’ll get much less than 10st., and
that Wilson will have a rare string to choose from, one of
the younger well-bred stable-mates or Trentham seems a
much more hopeful spec.
* * * *
As Carbine and Carnage both ran second in
Melb. Cup as 4-year-olds, it would only be in keeping with
family traditions if Wallace followed suit. However, it’s
quite on the cards that when his time comes he’ll run
second to none.
One explanation of the early spring wagering is
the public’s success at Randwick. In handing over
cheques Ikey Mo got in his little doubles by way of
solatium, and generous backers picked promiscuously.
Merely a case of tempering the wind to the shorn ram.
• * * *
The extra meeting of the A. J.C., within a week
of a four-days’ autumn carnival, seemed like a greedy
grasp at a well-filled public purse. Its success was mainly
due to people having plenty of ring-money to play with.
* * » *
The poor quality of Sydney hurdlers as a class
was again exemplified on Saturday, when old Escort,
dead-lame a few days previously, won, unbacked, in a
field of four.
* * * *
V.R.C. four-horse steeple on Saturday saw all
the horses fall over, the jockeys doing the chasing to have
another try. Abbotsford eventually got home. Remi-
niscent of a by-gone M.L. chase. The horse that got
furthest round stopped at the water for a full half-hour.
His win was due to a Chow, who threw a packet of lighted
crackers at his feet, after palings and stockwhips had
failed. The leap was a record one.
w * • *
Lillie Bridge’s ruling over the Compulsion-
Eurobin business was a quaint affair. Because the owner
won with the prad he had backed (Eurobin) in preference
to the favorite, the latter was disqualified, and the event
declared “no race.” Had W. R. Wilson elected, at the
last moment, to win Sydney Cup with Trentham instead
of Wallace, the public might have growled, but the club
couldn’t declare it “no race.” Lilliputians should be
governed by common-sense law. Small prad-owners
ought, in future, either advertise their intentions or tell
the stewards that their second strings carry the cash.
• • * *
Response another third—in April Stakes ! Dan
O’Brien has lost more over this beauty than he got when
he sold Carbine.
* • * ft
Wallace has already won a latter-day Melb. Cup
In point of value ; £5BBl to date.
• * * *
Following the Miller stable was a profitable
business at Flemington last Saturday. The red-and-white
jacket only got home once in five tries, but the winning
animal, Preston, started at 8 to 1. In the same race the
roguish Moorite, receiving 121 b. from Preston, was tried
again and found wanting.
» ♦ * •
A little tip, by way of sport,
For coming Melbourne Cup—
When they shall hang the numbers up,
Be sure you “ hold the Fort ! ”
* * * *
Vic. and S. A. bookies launched out early books
over the Onkaparinga meeting. The natty Dungan, the
Steeple favorite since the weights appeared, was backed
for thousands. This, on the top of the heavy Sydney Cup
blow, won’t leave Ikey Mo much spare cash for billiards.
* * * *
The straight-going brothers Manifold begin to
find honesty a non-paying racingpolioy. Two years back,
their b.g. Apsley won four hurdle-races in Melb., including
a G.N. Starting with a light impost, victory brought
such penalties that his fourth win found him with impos-
sible burdens. Dungan, the flying “ lepper,” is another of
Manifolds’ experiences. Once successful at Caulfield and
twice at Onkaparinga, his career seems as ended as
Apsley’s.
* * * *
Although S.A. police have instructions to arrest
“bookies” found wagering at horse-races, the broken-
nosed seem to do just as they please at local cycle meet-
ings. At S.A. Easter meeting the pavilions were crowded
with books, who openly cried the odds, whilst the police
were in full force at Tattersall’s races held on the same
day, ready to run in any of the small remnant in attend-
ance there. Wanted at S.A. cycle meets, either no betting
at all, or the tote !
• » * *
Ancient ’chaser Bushman, seen to advantage
lately, has retired twice, and is an old theatrical per-
former.
* * * *
Musket blood at Randwick meeting—nine firsts,
nine seconds and six thirds.
* ♦ * *
Vic. reps, have only won Sydney Cup thrice in
15 years—Carbine (twice) and his son.
* * * *
Fishhook, Petrea, Progress, and Wallace have
been the only three-year-olds to win the double, Sydney
Leger and Cup, in 30 years.
* * * *
As a result of the Musket-blood boom at ’Ome,
Petronel has advanced at a bound to lOOgs. per mare.
One sporting paper avers that, as Australian cracks are
inferior to the cream of English thoroughbreds, Petronel’s
claims to be considered the chief of his line are unde-
niable. Which is rough on Carbine and Trenton. Judg-
ing by a well-printed picture of Petronel, he and Manton
(N.S.W.) are more like Musket than any of the family.
The stock of the English rep. have, so far, been remark-
able for stamina rather than speed.
* * * *
The only S. Gup winner that ever carried 8.12
before Wallace was The Barb in ’6B, when Stumpy and
Tim Whiffler ran second and third. The following year
Tait’s black demon won with 10.8, and the same pair
finished behind him.
* * * *
Noticed that since good stakes have been offered
by leading S.A. clubs, tempting Vic. horses across, local
owners have had to be content with a very small piece of
pie.
* * * *
Bookies laying small boys the odds to three-
pence are a disgusting feature of Randwick outside en-
closures. Hominy should be meted out to all caught
taking coppers from youngsters over a horse-race.
* * * *
A country club, excised by the Wellington
Metrop. body under the turf-restriction policy, has caused
an unseemly tote squabble in M.L. by securing a tote
permit from the Premier, through influence. The Metrop.
club refused sanction to the fixture, and Mr. Seddon de-
clined to give the big club itself a permit until it backed
down. So there is no racing, and the club and the Pre-
mier are now “ standing on their dignity.” The Premier
is likely to stand the longer, but this row may mean the
abolition of the tote next session, and then
* * * »
Joe Thompson finds plenty of business for his
double, treble and quadruple event book, and had “ got
round ” marvellously well already, when the mail left
London.
• * * *
The latest turf imbecility. Buy a race-game,
name the mechanical tin neddies after prominent real
Mackays ; note the one that wins oftenest during the
evening, and back him for the Cup.
* * * *
It will be very fashionable ere long to send
youngsters ’Ome after they’ve shown form here. Fat
slakes and honor and glory are tempting baits to the
average long-purse owner.
An incident of the first Champion race, of ’59,
is recalled by Mr. Filgate’s death. He owned The Barber,
who, with Juniper, was sent to represent S.A., but got
wrecked in the Admella. The pair swam ashore, Juniper
with broken legs. Discovered a few days afterwards, The
Barber was taken on a week's walk to Melb., and started
hog-fat.
* * * *
Viewed in the murky light of past experience,
metrop. clubs are far more chary of passing out disqualified
neddies than the smaller institutions. They endorse the
latter’s periodical black-lists with the unction of men con-
scientiously performing a public duty, but their own cases
are treated differently. Suburban clubs do toss the flies
out of their cream-juy but leading - lights are
afraid to touch big owners. Influence and stiffness run
tandem fashion on the turf.
* * * *
Auraria, raced as a two-year-old right up to the
end of last season, when she won a mile and a quarter
event, only lasted over the Spring and collapsed, perhaps
for good. Youngsters aren’t moulded on cast-iron prin-
ciples.
* * * *
Nine thoroughbred horses fetched a total of =£69
under the hammer last week at Sydney. An £8 racehorse
is generally dearer than a whole shipful of poor, but affec-
tionate relations. Seeing that racing failures are at dog’s-
meat prices now-a-days, it’s a wonder racing men don’t
attempt to convert them into trotters. They’d fetch
perhaps treble their cost. The idea that a racer can’t trot
because he isn’t bred that way, and that he will always
want to bolt, is erroneous. The writer has seen any
number shaping well at the game, and the only neddy
that ever went full speed ahead started from scratch, and
caused the whole field to break. His whiskied jock had
his own ideas about making up ground, and, after finish-
ing on the other side of a furze hedge, was passed out for
a quarter—which period he spent in hospital.
* * * *
Old-fashioned Hinglish sport, on seeing Wal-
lace": “ Oh ! ’ee’s a chestnut, is ’ee. I carn’t stand ’ini.”
* * * *
A recent winner on a suburban course got home
through bolting in the straight when he didn’t oughter.
Next day, he was press-described as suddenly appearing
on the scene like “ a bolt from the blue.” His crowd
probably did look blue.
* * * *
Übiquitous Gollan, with horses in England,
M.L., and Sydney, deserves more than a casual bit of luck.
Tirb’s win at Randwick and Ebor’s at Wolverhampton was
a unique double. Ebor was always a top-storey hurdler,
but has only won twice in Gollan’s colors. A steeple was
thought foreign to his nature. “ If a prad can lep, put
him at ’em.”
* * • *
Wallace has inherited Carbine’s tendency to
hoof-splitting. The sire’s feet, as well as his teeth, were
troubling him when he carried lOst. into second place for
the Melb. Cup won by Bravo.
* * ♦ *
Surcingle: “Most of the principal jockeys
oppose the false _ rail. The crack artist prefers not to
have the professional dangers minimised, like the Sheffield
knife-grinder.”
* * * »
G. H. Clifford, Major George, and G. S.
Stead, three M.L. turf pillars, simultaneously announced
their retirement. Then Stead scored four wins and five
places at Christchurch meeting. Again the devil’s whis-
per ! Stead couldn’t get prices when his cracks were
auctioned, and so kept them.
* * *
Lord Byron (Neckersgat—Fille-de-Joie), winner
of V.R.C. April Hurdle-race, is well enough bred to win a
Melb. Cup. Brewer rode a wonderful race, and just
squeezed home by a neck.
* * * *
The unwritten history of some of this season’s
big cycle handicaps would be interesting. As an instance
of the value of pacing, one back-mark man (an even-
money favorite in a field of 12) agreed to divide equally
with a rider (also among the back division) to pace him
through. The stake was one of the largest yet offered,
and the last-named rider had not a 25 to 1 chance of even
getting a place from his mark, in his then condition. It
was a great race, and the favorite could only get second
money. This—in itself a decent stake—was equally
divided with the pacemaker, who lasted long enough to
get his man up to the field.
In another valuable handicap the winner re-
ceived just 8-25ths of the total first-prize money. The
trainer, a man who can dictate his own terms, received a
third (besides a regular weekly payment), and the rest
went in “cuts” to pacers. As a one-time pedestrian, the
present writer, like many others, could not understand the
great advantage of pacing until he took to cycle racing.
Then it became easily apparent: there is a great differ-
ence between running a mile in 4.45 against a strong wind
and riding a bicycle against the same wind at a 2.25 bat.
Besides which there is the moral support of “ something
to go at”—not any disadvantage, even in pedestrianism.
* * * *
It looks as if Peter Jackson is a back number,
The razor-edged Pony Moore has offered to back Charley
Mitchell, Paddy Slavin, or an unknown against him.
* * * *
A sign of the times. The Sydney dailies announce
that bike ads. will be classified in a special column.
* * * *
As showing the extent of the cycle craze, it is
cabled that a syndicate has boug'ht the pneumatic tire
co.’s business for £3,000,000. The co.’s original capital
was £22,500, and in a few years £658,000 has been paid in
divs. The scheme is to refloat for six millions—the figure
reached by Guinness’s brewery.
* * * *
Martin should really have broken his neck in
the two-mile at Adelaide Easter cycling sports. He was
making the pace a cracker w'hen his tyre punctured and
the crack turned a complete somersault, Harris riding-
right over him.
* * * *
A. C. Nesbitt, an English cyclist, is said to have
ridden one mile, unpaced, on the main road, in lmin.
25 2-ssec.
» * ♦ *
Last Saturday’s racing convinced sceptics that
the Victorian wonder, Parsons, will rank as a second or
third-rater amongst European and American speedmen,
as he was, in turn, defeated by Rice, Porta, Megson, and
Harris—three times over a mile and once over five miles—
each of them having an equal amount of racing.
* * * *
The Melbourne bike reporters put in an excuse
for Parsons every time he gets beaten. He was always a
certain winner, until something happened to “give” the
victory to the other man, always presuming that the
other man was Martin, Porta, or Harris. The young
man will sorely miss this loyal barracking when he goes
abroad.
* * * *
Yankee Martin made himself decidedly nasty
over his mishap in the final heat of the Parsons Handi-
cap, alleging that sundry riders had conspired to bring
him down. Appearances didn’t favor the plugger’s com-
plaint, but some allowance can be made for the feelings
of a man who has just come a cropper from his bike and
been damaged in the head by a passing pedal.
Harris’s performances at the Parsons’ benefit
meeting in Melbourne suggested that the blonde Britisher
had been celebrating his recent return to “ form.” There
was no vigor in his sprint.
In the Parsons Handicap, Martin picked up the
field with a fine run, and then got pocketed. “ Martin in
the centre ; look out for the fall! ” said a spectator, who
had suffered by proximity to “ Plugger Bill ” in previous
races. Just then it came ; Martin bringing himself and a
section of the bunch down. Martin and Porta are most
execrable riders to compete against, both from want of
control of their high-geared machines, and from the reck-
less way in which they bustle for a chance to break
through.
* * * *
What about Zimmerman’s much-published
assertion that Parsons had proved himself superior to
Harris? Zim. either talked nonsense with malice afore-
thought, or else he was really as dull as he looked. The
Bulletin, at the time, wanted to know which Harris the
’Murkan “ authority ” was referring to —the man in con-
dition or altogether out of it. Both of them at their
best, Harris is considerably faster and stronger than
Parsons.
* * * *
The prolonged sprint with which Megson wore
Harris and co. down in the International Mile, at the
Parsons meeting, was the most brilliant (bar those of
Zimmerman) yet seen in Australia.
* * * •
The proposed Harris-Martin match climaxed in
the dressing-room at the Parsons meeting; a debate
between Martin and Harris’s trainer winding up in a set.
to, in which got badly worsted. It took
three able-bodied men to keep the Yank, off his victim
after the latter’s collapse. Martin, by the way, with his
moustache shaven, now presents a more Sioux-like ap-
pearance than ever, and, with a few feathers stuck m ms
hair, would be “Sitting Bull” or “Spotted Tail to
the life.
* * * *
Ex-sprinter Darrell is the first gentleman of
color to compete under the Vic. Cycle League s rules.
The unbleached American gets to the front rapidly, but
has not been long enough at the game to stay throughout
a fast-run race. Darrell is a perfect sft. Hercules, weigh-
ing list. 51b., without a superfluous ounce.
* * * *
Premier Turner’s son (confined, by the parental
edict, to the amateur class) has run first and second, from
scratch, in two consecutive races. With systematic
training he could soon be brought back amongst the first-
class professional division.
Betting is rampant at Melb. cycling meetings,
and the odds are cried by at least a score of petty book-
makers. The average number of starters in a handicap
heat is about a dozen, and it is always—“ 2tol on the
field !” Then some fatuous youth puts a half-crown on his
fancy. This at once establishes a favorite, and the cry
becomes, “6to 4 Pedals ;2to 1 on the field !” Someone
else puts a half-crown on Crank, which instantly sends
the betting to “2to 1, bar two.” However, people must
have “ something on,” and several of the best known of
the “shilling” books are doing much better at the ridi-
culous odds offered and taken than they can at even
suburban race swindles.
* * * *
Yankee files announce a projected mile cycle v.
train race. A sexplet is being built to travel 100 miles an
hour. Latest calculations show that the highest speed is
attainable with six riders, all above that number being
dead-weight. In the new machine each man is provided
with an air reservoir, and breathes through specially
designed tubes. With differentiated gear up to 150 the
sexplet will need to run a mile to get up speed, and two
more before it can be stopped. The man who falls off will
see stars.
* * * *
That modest young man, Mr. Percy Hunter,
late of Sydney D.T., now edits a Melb. cycling journal,
the fact being displayed on the cover in large caps. In
the current number the name of Mr Percy Hunter occurs
no less than seven times, a speech of his is awarded a
column with “ applause ” liberally interspersed and a por-
trait of his visage occupies a prominent position on a
central page.
* * * *
Harry K. Williams, of the S.M. Herald staff,
completed a cycling trip from Sydney to Melb. through
Snowy River district, “Banjo’s” country, during the
Easter Holidays. The journey has been done only once
before, by George Thorne in 1889, and is a heartrending
job from a cyclist’s point of view.
* * * *
The sculling-match (<£loo a-side) fixed for Aug.
between rising George Towns and plucky Chris. Neilsen
has a genuine sporting flavor Towns, only 21, has a
vague idea of being world’s champion some day. If he
doesn’t beat the Dutchman his prospects are about equal
to those of a camel in a hurdle-race.
* * * *
Stanbury will invite all ’Murka to “come on ”
if he downs Harding. As ’Murka consists mostly of the
Canadian Hanlan, it’s 2 to 1 the ancient will respond.
He’d reckon on making a good show, too, despite j’ears of
an adulterated-lemonade tendency. Stanbury should
beat Harding on July 13, unless he joins an Orange pro-
cession the day previous, and gets hit on the head with a
brick.
* * * *
Australia may now gradually prepare itself for a
daily cable surfeit re the adventures of Aus. XI., English
press opinions, and what willow stars think and don’t
think. Also, interesting items about Giffen’s sciatica.
* * * *
Editor Bulletin, —The speedy Ettie is not an
amateur-built boat. The Ettie was not built by “ Billy ”
Wain, but by his brother “ Bob,” boss boat-builder in the
Port-office yard. She was built in a backyard, at Bulimba,
under candle-light. “Bob ” Wain, in making her model,
was not guided by any other boat or person ; and he has
introduced a distinct style of craft. Sydney will have a
chance of seeing her in Nov. —J.D.
* * * *
Despite N.S.W. reverses, Melb. Rugby foot-
ballers expect the coming season to boom their game.
Many converts have been captured ; one of the finest foot-
ball trophies ever offered locally is to be played for ; and,
as M.L. and English teams are expected over, things look
rosy for the game down south.
• * * *
Sydney Rugby Union’s scheme to send a team
’Ome in ’97 should stir up the football world this winter.
A few leaves from the management book of the Maori
team will require perusal and thorough revision. Cold
day heroes require an iron curb. An appalling thirst
develops the bump of continuity, and, from a festive
standpoint, places them on a par with a carol-singing
band out on a Christmas prowl.
♦ * * *
An inventive Vie. civil servant seems to be
doing well out of his bicycle, the chief apparent advan-
tage of which is the comparatively easy working of its
high-gear and a decided facility for climbing big hills.
The rider’s position is altogether novel, standing rather
than sitting on the machine ; he also gets the extra
advantage of a glorified back-kick at the space he -has just
left behind. Another successful inventor is a Vic. Govt,
engineer, whose flying-machine is now being considered
by the British Admiralty. Ingenious arrangements for
counteracting specific gravity and effective steering-gear
constitute the main differences from other flying appara-
tus. Prof. Kernot is said to hold that the Australian has
at last successfully solved the flying problem.

Familiar names in a N.S.W. country race entry-
list—lkey Mo and Simple Jim.
ON THE ROAD—AT EASTER.
We watched them, doubled on their cycles,
whisking,
As if their lives depended on the pace ;
A metal road, fair wind, no small boys frisking
With tacks, or hooks, and sinister grimace.
They might have been in Mercury’s winged
sandals,
So swift the party travelled on the road,
With bodies bent, and firmly gripped the handles,
In suits of grey and felt hats —a la mode.
But worse than Hercules’ twelve labors, tracing
The outlines of these figures, to define
The men’s and girls’—that, innocent of lacing,
Displayed no angles or artistic line.
Their backs were much alike, no sign revealing,
The limbs, a has, were cased in leggings stout,
Defying every thrust of curious feeling
Tq prove the sex and settle points of doubt.
And as they wheeled along the track together
We looked, in vain, for frills or epaulets,
But found some hats distinguished by a feather,
And sundry knobs confined in back-hair nets.
It seems that nevermore in manner tricky
Our girls will live to graduate as flirts,
But, with a stoic heart beneath their dicky,
Develope muscle in divided skirts.
April 7.
EUGENIA.

ever since with swollen veins, and

should be obliged to give up my

after taking four bottles the swell-

1874. 1895.
In This my Twenty-first Year I Have Ascertained Just
to What Extent the Pioneer of All Type Writers Out
Numbers the Combined Forces of Competition in New
York City—the Home and Headquarters of Type Writers.
For the Past 16 Years Attempt After Attempt Has Been
Made to Displace Me in the Estimation of the Public,
Each Succeeding Year Producing More Than Its Pre-
decessors of Would Be Rivals. Many Have Fallen by
the Wayside. Of the Survivors No Less Than 26 Different
Makes Are To-day Represented in the New York Market.
A Complete Census of Writing Machines in Use in New
York City is Impracticable, But in View of the Prevailing
Tendency to Concentration of Business in the Large Office
Buildings, I Now Account for the Type Writers in Use in
78 PER CENT. REMINGTON.
A total of 3426 Type Writers (of 26 different makes) in
actual operation in the 34 leading office buildings of New
York City.

Thus a Hobart W.C.T.U. lady lecturer on
Woman Suffrage:—
Our brothers have a great aversion to what is often
jeeringly called the “New Woman.” 1 wonder if we
ought to take it as a compliment, or to mean they are
quite satisfied with women as they are? Well, we are not
satisfied with ourselves, and very often we are not satisfied
with them, and would gladly welcome, a “New Man.”
Then there was a chorus of shrill treble “hear
hears,” and the males in the audience looked un-
comfortable.
Parrots are terribly liable to tuberculosis,
and as pets are a greater menace to a household
than a hundred tuberculous cows. Of 154 sick
parrots treated at an English veterinary institute
last year, no less than 50 (or 36 per cent.) had tuber-
culosis, with Koch’s bacillus well developed. The
other day a doctor, called in to see a case of con-
sumption in a little girl, when the family history
was perfectly free from that disease, noticed the
pet parrot was ailing. The parrot was found to
have tuberculosis, and the problem of the girl’s
case was solved. “ Kiss pretty Joey ? ” No!
Dear Bulletin, —How does Dr. Fiaschi
escape the provisions of the Foreign Enlistment
Act which, in the case of his Scottish colleague,
Dr. “ Jim ” and whilom brother-filibusterer, are
about to be brought into operation with possibly
dire effect ? Dr. Fiaschi, holding H.M’s. commis-
sion as Surgeon-Captain in N.S.W. Cavalry, must
be a naturalized subject and have taken the oath
of allegiance ; therefore, he owes no patriotism to
any country but the one he resides in, and of
which he has deliberately elected to become a
citizen. Great Britain is not at war with Abys-
sinia ; if, therefore, a British subject joins the
Italians in their unholy attack upon Abyssinia,
how does he escape the charge of filibustering
within the meaning of the Foreign Enlistment
Act ? By the way, I see it stated that this Act
was passed in connection with Fenian raids into
Canada in 1870, yet, anterior to this, Lord Dun-
donald Cochrane was obliged to reside on the
Continent for a considerable time in order to
escape conviction under that Act for waging
war against Spain in the South American
Republics, and, later on, on behalf of so-
called Greek patriots. Cochrane’s case was even
less conspicuons than those of Drs. “Jim”
and Fiaschi, for he held no commission in the
service of Great Britain, and was a naturalized
subject of Chili, in which country he had acquired
property—nay, he had been struck off the navy-
list and, as it were, expatriated by a cruel and un-
just decision that deprived the country of the
services of a sea-king second not even to Nelson
himself. This, to his friends, appeared ample
justification for his actions, which, ultimately,
were pardoned. The loss to N.S.W. of a prac-
titioner of the standing and learning of Dr.
Fiaschi is in itself bad enough, but when it is
occasioned by a series of errors which may end in
even worse disaster to the militant surgeon him-
self, then it is deplorable. So far as we know,
Abyssinia is as yet a friendly power ; how, then,
(without coming under the Foreign Enlistment
Act) can any British subject join the Italian army
for the avowed purpose of fighting a friendly
power, even though that power be only an Ethio-
pian one? —Banshee. [Lord Dundonald’s case
was under a different Act. How does Fiaschi’s
case go with that of Hobart Pasha? Also, does
the Foreign Enlistment Act apply to the man
who enlists himself or the one who enlists other
people? Also, does it matter? —Ed. B.]

Mr. Button writes later: —“The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
In one case, when three hunters and trappers
were travelling about the ranges near my place on
the Rose River, one was taken very ill ; his mates
intended taking him in a waggon to Wangaratta
(about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor. I
gave him a few of your Pills. lam pleased to
inform you that having to pass their camp next
day, I found him up and pegging-out his skins
and looking well. This speaks volumes for your
valuable Remedies.”

In a recent London law-suit, it was testi-
fied by experts that the average retail profit on
cigarettes was 75 per cent., on pipes and fancy-
goods 50, tobacco 20, British cigars 33 to 60, and
foreign cigars 33 per cent. “ The price of a cigar
depended, not on the taste of a smoker, but on the
position of the shop.”
The South African “sensitive plant” is
gaining a fim hold in Southern Queensland. The
Queenslanders view it as an interesting little
curiosity. Thus Stevenson re the same plant in
Samoa:—“A fool brought it over in a pot and
used to sentimentalise over the tender thing ; but
the tender thing has now taken charge of the
island, and strong men fight it with torn hands
for bare life.”

The realities of Australia, the Workman’s
Paradise Lost. “It was this way with me,” said
Tom, the bush-whacker, as he laid down his axe
for a smoke. “ Old man says to me ‘ what’s the
good of you slavin’ here for a paltry 15 shillin’s a
week ? Go to Australia and get your four pound
a week, and perhaps make your fortune.’ So I
came; landed in Melbourne with ten notes.
Didn’t know much of the country then or I
wouldn’t have busted it the way I did, in four
days. I had some pretty good togs on, so I
swapped ’em for a swag and billy and tramped off
to get a job at four notes a week. Walked about
for three months before I got a job. And it was a
job—slavin’ from four o’clock in the morning till
seven at night for ten bob a week. A chap on the
same place told me to save my money and go and
fet work through a labor-office in town. I did.
’aid a pound for the job and spent my last stiver
in the railway-fare. When I’d gone 150 miles by
train and walked another 30, I found there was no
job at all. Walked a few more hundred miles,
and landed at last, hanging in tatters, and half
starved, on another ten-bob-a-week job. Got the
sack the third week because I couldn’t split rails
quick enough. Picked up another billet in a few
days—eight bob a week this time. That was the
hardest work I ever had. One day the boss (he
was a cocky and quarryman combined) put me
feeding a stone crusher with an eleven-pronged
fork. I struck, and humped it once again. _ Took
a spell as slushy in a shanty after this at six bob
a week. Been two and a half years in the country
now and never got more than 12s. 6d. a week. I
ought to have known better than take advice from
the old man.”
When Rockhampton first arose on the
banks of Fitzroy an allotment was granted to the
Church of England, but, up to Separation time,
no minister of any denomination had settled
there, neither was there any school, so the
people subscribed and erected a building on this
allotment, which was used as a school on week-
days and as a church on Sundays. If a minister
of any kind visited Rockhampton he held the ser-
vice of his church in it, but, if there was no minis-
ter in town, the clerk of petty-sessions read the
Anglican service. However, after Separation,
Bishop Tufnell, of Brisbane, visited Rockhampton,
and was indignant that other denominations were
allowed to hold service in the building, which was
on C. of E. land. He preached in the church next
Sunday, and announced that no minister of any
other creed would in future be permitted to hold
service in it. As all had subscribed to the erection
of the place, people were very angry, and the com-
mittee by whom it had been built determined to
pull it down and put it up elsewhere. But, man
proposes and God disposes,” for, just after the
children had left the school on Monday, a heavy
squall levelled the edifice to the ground.

Victoria will have to do something desper-
ate with its Water Commission. Some time in
the long ago the Patterson Ministry appointed
certain of its hangers-on to go round the country
at the public expense, and report on the water and
irrigation trusts. The report was to be forth-
coming in three months ; but for two solid years
the Commission has gone to and fro, and consumed
much drink, solid food, and cigars, arid looked
with unseeing eye at irrigation ditches in all parts
of the country, and no report is forthcoming.
Finally, the Turner Ministry cut off the supplies
and left the Commission to pay its own food and
liquor bill; but it still wanders to and fro from
force of habit, and doesn’t report. Premier Turner
now thinks of having the Commission arrested for
vagrancy.
Torresian divers are hard to manage. The
other day a lugger was fitted out with extra piping
because the Manilla diver wished to go into deep
shell-bearing water. ,£l3 in advance did the
Manilla man receive ; a crew was specially fitted
out total outfit, about £BO. After five weeks the
boat returned with one pair shell valued at about
Is. 6d. The diver was afraid to go down in the
deep water.
Ihe Argus wants private ownership of
water, and points out that the Vic. fisheries are
being destroyed because water is “no man’s land.”
It will be a happy day for Fatman when the
lower orders ” have to supplicate him for the
privilege of having a square wash. At present,
Britannia rules the waves merely ; in the ordinary
course of events, she will syndicate them.

Waiting!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He kissed the children as they slept, took
up his bag of Government rations, and, stepping
gently, went on tip-toe to the door. His wife was
there.
Good bye, Sarah,” he said, with a voice half
choking. I will write to you soon.”
“ I’m afraid, Hal, I’m afraid you are not fit for
bush work ; you know you are not. Mind you
come back at once if you have no luck.”
Never you worry, old woman. A change is
bound to come. I should have left this starving
Sydney life long ago. The bush is the place for a
man of spirit; thousands of miles of beautiful
land, undug and untrodden. Why, there must be
room for everybody there. Think of it, Sarah !
A little bark hut all our own, with a cow and pigs
and poultry, and a garden for vegetables, and—
and—no rent to pay ! ”
The man’s sad eyes flashed the joy his mind had
conjured up. The woman pretended to have faith,
and she kissed him long and softly on each cheek.
Then, standing in the doorway, she watched his
jaunty step gradually falter at the bending of the
road into the dragging walk of a spiritless man.
* * * *
A month had gone by since Labor Bureau
free-passes had deposited Henry M'Kinnon and a
score of other destitutes at a back-block township.
Sydney was well rid of this eyesore to her beau-
tiful streets. How many miles had MTvinnon
travelled since he left the railway-station ? It
seemed, to his unpractised feet, thousands and
thousands. His rations exhausted, for days he had
Hot lived, but existed on the casual meal given
at a wayside hut. If he had had more “cheek ”
he would have done better. If the roads had not
swarmed with others like himself, seeking the
work that was not to be found, the generosity of
the ordinary bushman would have supplied his
daily bread. “ But what can we do?” said one
Woman to him, as she cut him a crust off her last
loaf. “ If there was one there has been a dozen
swagmen call here this morning.” For three days he
had eaten nothing but a piece of raw pumpkin. One
foot was so blistered and swollen that he could not
put the boot on, and limped along with it swathed
in old rags and stringy-bark. He had no heart
now to go off the road and call at the houses, here
few and far between ; he plodded on wearily,
aimlessly, he knew not where. He had only
walked seven miles that day when he camped at
nightfall by the roadside.
“ Starving’s not so bad as they make out,” he
said to himself as he sat bathing his swollen foot;
“it was a bit rough yesterday, but to-day I feel
pretty right. If I could only get a smoke ! ”
“ Good evening, mate ! I’ll camp here,” and
the new-comer, unslinging his swag, sat down.
“ What, got a bad foot ? Not used to the road, I
can see. A bloke wants to know the ropes when
he humps his bluey, my oath ! ”
“ Can you find work, then ? ”
“Yakker be damned! I’ve never done er
tap for 20 year. Not such a mug. Ain’t
there hundreds an’ hundreds as is beggitv and
praying fer graft, and de yer think I’m dog enough
ter rob ’em of what I don’t want ? Got any
tucker ? ”
“ No,” said M‘Kinnon.
“ No more have I, worse luck ! This is one of
my off days, but I’ve got ’bacca enough for two. ”
The swagman proceeded*to cut up the remnant of
a plug, and, filling his own pipe and M’Kinnon’s,
®'™ ss and supperless they smoked.
• yon what it is, mate. The country is
just going to the devil, you make no error. I
know the time when a bloke was always right for
a square feed without asking. Now you have to
tell ein pretty straight you’ve got a mouth on
yer.”
NV hat s to be done?’’said M’Kinnon, feebly
interested.
1 here s too many of us, that’s a fact. I always
said, and always will say, that if fifty thousand
men of real grit liked to do it, they could make
the country as good as ever.’’
“ Do what? ”
Go and hang themselves. The country is all
right, it’s only over stocked. What we want is a
good boil-down of surplus labor.”
morning, when the svvagman left him,
* ii mnon was too stiff and full of pains to stir.
All that day he sat there, in the Jsame place, his
back resting against a blackbutt. Only when he
moved did he feel pains. The griping pangs in his
empty stomach troubled him no longer, and in its
place a fever warmed his limbs and fired his skin.
His mind reverted, with a species of fascination,
to the proposal of his late companion, for the im-
molation of the workless workers for the benefit of
their fellows.
“ Why not ? ” he asked himself. “ What are we
good for, anyhow ? A misery to ourselves, a
nuisance to others, unwelcome visitors. Why do
we not take the hint and go? For reasons we do
not understand, this world does not want us just
now—she gives us the sack. Why not be men,
and step out before we are kicked out ? ”
Then his mind wandered on to the memory of
his wife and children, and thoughts of himself
melted away in the doubts and misgivings of what
they had suffered and were suffering. He pictured
them in that bare, gaunt cottage, living on the
dole of charity; he noted the look in his wife’s
face as her pride revolted under the yoke she
carried; he marked his eldest girl, who wondered
why she had no boots to go to school; and so they
passed before him with the clearness of delirium,
and tears came from his eyes —he could look no
more.
The night had come, and with the night a howl-
ing rain. It poured, sheeted down in masses ; the
road was a river, the bush a lake, but the man
heeded it not. The tree was no shelter to him ;
even where he sat, a pool of water formed. Per-
haps the water cooled his hot limbs. His mind
travelling backwards had, by the natural progres-
sion of ideas, arrived at that moment, a month
ago, when he pictured the day-dream to his wife.
A bark hut with a little garden, a cow, a pig,
and poultry—all his own—and his mind, losing all
coherence, dwelt on this ideal and added details of
imposing splendor. Hives of bees, fruit-trees,
a paddock with a horse in it —all was plain to
him, through the rain that streamed off his old hat
on to his knees ; through the inky blackness of the
night his burning eyes saw clearly and more clearly.
The hut was there quite close to him. His wife
was at the door, rosy and well-clothed. They had
seen him at the gate and his youngsters were run-
ning, shouting, down the path to meet him. He
caught Bob up in his arms and danced him in the
air, the child’s eyes laughing back gladness to him.
* * * *
Very few readers of the papers noticed in the
country telegrams the item from Waler.
The police report the finding of the dead bod}’ of a
swagman, name unknown, on the Sydney road. At the
coroner’s inquest a verdict, in accordance with the medi-
cal evidence, was returned that death had been caused by
want and exposure. A rider was added by the jury that
somethin# should be done to abate the nuisance caused
by the orowds of destitute tramps that infested the dis-
trict.
* * * *
In a cottage at Marrickville, too damp for
a rent-paying tenant to live in, a sad-faced woman
and her children are ever at the window when the
postman passes on his rounds. The gleam of hope
in their eyes brightens only to fade, as he passes
unheeding by. They are waiting.
HENRY FLETCHER.
In Flood Time.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
For twenty-four hours it had rained unceasingly.
The creeks that had stopped during the drought
were now swollen to miniature rivers. It was
getting towards midnight, and, save a light in the
“Squatters’ Arms,” darkness reigned over the
little township. The mail for Barwonga was
ready. The five horses stood harnessed to the
heavy coach and drooped their heads in the falling
rain.
‘ ‘ Any passengers ? ” asked the driver.
“ One, a woman. Says her husband is a shearer
—dying at Black Johnson’s pub.”
“I don’t believe I’ll get across the river; it
must be a banker by this. I wouldn’t be sur-
prised if I have to put in a couple of days at
Bogan’s.”
“But you’ll try and get there, won’t you? I
must see him before he dies,” said a young woman
at the door.
The driver promised to do his best, and getting
his solitary passenger in the coach he picked up
his mail-bags at the office and was soon on the
lonely journey to Barwonga. For a long while
nothing was heard but the noise of the coach and
the falling rain, and vacantly the driver stared
over the heads of the leaders into the darkness
beyond the light of the big coach lamps.
A voice roused him.
“ Will you please let me come outside ? It’s so
lonely here.”
“ But you will be soaked in five minutes,” re-
monstrated the driver.
“That would be better than sitting here alone
with my sad thoughts.”
The driver gave way, and covered her up with
an old oil-skin. “That’s Bogan’s,” he said; “if
the river is too high we’ll have to come back here. ”
“ But you promised to try,” pleaded the young
wife.
The driver answered “Very well,” and shook
his horses up as they descended the long hill that
led to the river. It was pitch dark, and only an
accustomed eye could have told where the crossing-
place was. He handed the reins to his companion,
and, taking one of the lamps, walked to the edge.
He came back slowly.
“ It’s no go,” he said. “ When the stump in the
middle of the river’s covered it’s not safe. I’m
very sorry for you ; but I can’t risk another man’s
coach and horses, so I’ll have to turn them round.”
There was a minute’s silence. Then a deter-
mined voice said, “ Could one horse swim it? ”
“He might, if he weren’t hooked to anything.”
“ Will you take one out and I will ride him
across. ”
The driver stared. He was no coward ; he had
been in many a tight place; but he didn’t care
about facing the river in its present state.
“I’ll not go back,” she said. “ You may either
lend me a horse or leave me here.”
The driver thought a bit. “I’ll do neither.
If you were a man I’d leave you to your chances,
but as you’re a woman we’ll cross or go down
together.”
He shifted his mail-bags higher, and grasped the
reins. The woman clasped her hands and turned
her face heavenwards.
The leaders snorted, but he forced them into it
with whip and voice.
Between his shut teeth he said: “I haven’t
much faith in prayers, but you can throw in one
for me ; and if you feel the coach going jump clear
of the horses.”
The river was coming down with tremendous
force. For a moment the driver reckoned it was
all up. He could hear the water rushing through
the bottom of the coach, and the horses were
struggling for footing. He was about to cry
“ Jump ! ’’ when the leaders began to ascend, and
in a few minutes the water was only at the axles.
He turned to the woman just in time to stop her
from falling. She had fainted.
It was early morning in the Selectors’ Hotel,
and Black Johnson was leaning on the bar.
“ That shearer cove keeps asking about the
coach. Wants to know if Nelly’s come. There’ll
be no coach to-day unless the Devil drives. . . ,
What! It’s coming ? ”
“ Yes, and a stranger on the box.”
“ The Devil ! ”
“ No, Jack Duggan and a woman.”
But when the driver told his story he put the
woman first.
HUGH STONE.
Alleged by naturalist Milligan, of Traral-
gon, Vic., that the female Australian cuckoo is
polyandrous ; also, that female cuckoos and laugh-
ing jackasses often adopt stray youngsters in the
open country, or even feed caged youngsters with
choice morsels through the bars.
The serum treatment scores big successes
in leprosy. Sensibility returns rapidly to anaes-
thetic areas; the pigmented spots lose much. of
their colour ; the bloating much diminishes, and
bloated spots become almost normal; the tubercles
flatten, become soft, and disappear sometimes by
absorption, sometimes by suppuration; ulcerations
rapidly cicatrise and heal up ; the ‘ ‘ leonine
aspect ”is entirely lost; appetite, sleep, hope and
contentment return.
Dear Bulletin, —Your “ road-mending ”
farmer is all over N.S.W. The members are put
in by cockie “ road-menders.” And these same
contractors and muck-eaters have much to con-
tend against. In the small towns the J’s.P. take
such jobs in their sons’ names. In one, the farms
are snapped up by the local inspector of stock, as
are some of the show prizes. Tenders for police
and hospital wood are cut down in exactly the
same way. And still they “go on the land.”
But the Germans and Chinese and other aliens
don’t leave their farms for such work, and they
certainly eat better tucker than white men. I
have thought the matter over, and, after tackling
Freetrade, Protection, Federation, bimetallism,
State bank and State monopoly, I can only find
one exit to the difficulty and that is “ threepenny
drinks ”in the bush. That alone would leave a
balance of one-third of a man’s wages for house-
hold expenses. Yes, sir, let us have threepenny
beers, and big ones at that, and prosperity will
again reign.— Scotty the Wrinkler.

Chateau Wunghnu.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The North Wind shaking
His torch on high
All folk is making
Extremely dry ;
The Town is baking
As ’twere a pie.
The City-Dweller
Both breathes and sees
This Dust-Compeller,
This Tophet breeze,
While in cool cellar
We lounge at ease.
We call the waiter,
“ Wunghnu ” demand :
The shirt-sleeved Satyr
Won’t understand ;
He brings—the traitor !
A foreign brand.
Plague take these young, new
Hands where one dines,
He thinks our tongue new—
Welsh he opines—
“ Bring Chateau Wunghnu l
The King of Wines / ”
He comes—proceeding
With step of stork—
Forth corkscrew ! speeding ,
Like lightning-fork,
It draws out bleeding
The faithful cork.
Now, no more juggling,
O Waiter droll!
The bottle, struggling
To reach the bowl,
With rich glug-gluggling
Gives up its soul.
See how it flashes,
This grape-blood fine !
Our beards it splashes,
O comrade mine !
Life dust and ashes
Were, wanting wine.
Amontillado
Fires heart and eyes;
Champagne the shadow
Of care defies;
An Eldorado
In Rhine-wine lies;
Port’s ever ready
For gen’rous deeds ;
Tokay, though heady,
Right nobly bleeds ;
But as a steady
Drink Wunghnu leads.
Yet is it wanting
In poesy:
No legends haunting
Its vassals be,
No tales enchanting
Of chivalry.
Spain’s grape hath stories :
Its blood the bold
Conqui stadores
Drank deep of old —
A wine of glories,
A wine of gold.
Who drinks, not sparing,
Beholdeth he
The great Cid bearing
His banner free,
Columbus daring
The unknown Sea;
And, haply biding
In this dream—Spain,
Don Quixote riding
Across the plain,
His squire confiding
Beside his rein.
The wine of France is
Aglow to-day
With flash of lances,
With feast and fray,
And dark-eyed glances
Of ladies gay.
See where together,
A flagon near,
Lie hat with feather,
And long rapier
Fine courting weather,
O Cavalier !
Bright Rhenish, gleaming
Moon-white ! Perchance
Thy wave clear-beaming
Still guards Romance,
Not dead, but dreaming
In spell-bound trance !
Not in Rhine-water,
But Rhine-wine fair,
Sir Rupert sought her—
(As bards declare)
The Elf-King’s daughter
With golden hair.
Still ’neath its smiling
Wave’s amber rings,
Men sweetly wiling
From earthly things,
Her song beguiling
The Loreley sings.
Your cup, wild siren,
That Deutschland drains—
Her heart of iron
Moved by your strains—
No blood shall fire in
Australian veins ;
Nor yours whose charm is
Your topaz eyne,
Nor yours whose armies
In gold-caps shine,
Shall charm or harm us—
Eh, comrade mine ?
No vintage alien
For thee or me !
Our fount Castalian
Of poesy
Shall wine Australian,
None other be.
Then place your hand in
This hand of mine,
And while we stand in
Her brave sunshine
Pledge deep our land in
Our land’s own wine.
It has no glamour
Of old romance;
Of war, and armour,
In Spain or France ;
Its poets stammer
As yet, perchance;
But he may wholly
Become a seer
Who quaffs it slowly ;
For he shall hear,
Though faintly, lowly,
Yet sweet and clear
The axes ringing
On mountain sides,
The wool-boats swinging
Down Darling tides,
The drovers singing
Where Clancy rides,
The miners driving,
The stockman’s strife;
All sounds conniving
To show the rife,
Rich, rude, strong-striving
Australian life.
Once more your hand in
This hand of mine !
And, while we stand in
The brave sunshine,
Pledge deep our land in
Our land’s own wine !
VICTOR J. DALEY.

The N.S.W. Public Service Board, writes
a Bulletin correspondent, deserves well at the
hands of a long-suffering public, if only for its
hardihood in tackling, and finally bursting, the
notoriously-close religious ring which, in days gone
by, bossed the entrance to a certain ridiculously-
over-manned Govt, department. The writer re-
members with a shudder the solemn churchy atmo-
sphere which pervaded that office and all its doings
in its halcyon days. Everything about the place
bore testimony to the preternatural holiness of its
officials. The very light, streaming in, softly
touched their heads and endowed them with fan-
tastic haloes. The ledgers were not the greasy,
dog-eared vols. to be met with in other Govt,
offices —they had the sanctified appearance of
bibles, and the officials who reverently turned over
their pages wore the smug look of recording
angels. The officers were all dressed in seedy
black,- of the dullest and most sombre hue, save at
the seams and shoulders, which shone like glory.
One felt assured that when they arrived at office
in the morning they pawed each other with
cold, clammy hands, and asked if they were still
Saved, and, perhaps, sang a hymn and had prayers.
Anyhow, they were a snug family, cocksure of a
clear receipt at the final audit when the recon-
structed banks shall finally pay their depositors.
At mid-day they sat apart reading tracts, secretly
pecking at their lunches, and swilling large quan-
tities of cold water. They never spoke, but sniffed
constantly. Even on the office-boy had the un-
earthly blight fallen—he sat alone in a corner
reading a volume of the Rev. E. P. Roe’s, in lieu
of “ Dead wood Dick.”
An Idyll .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The ball-room at Government House is filled with
a brilliantly - plumaged throng. Their Good
Graciousnesses have just entered to the triumphal
strains of the National Anthem, and are distri-
buting nods, smiles, and handshakes to the grate-
ful crowd. White throats and bosoms gleam in
the lamplight—there is a glitter and sparkle of
innumerable diamonds, and here and there a
uniform makes a splash of color amid the sober
black of civilian dress.
A magnificent woman sails into the room. Smil-
ing, decolletee, spick and span from her diamond
tiara to the tip of her satin shoe, Mrs. Tom
Scourings is a perfect type of the well-developed
human animal. As poor little Tom follows her
up the room, he is ridiculously suggestive of a
Manly ferry-boat puffing up the harbor in the
wake of aP.& O. liner. She is soon surrounded
by a crowd of young men —and you may be sure
that little Freddy Tremayne, the A.D.C., is not
far away. He is a smart-looking little chap
is Freddy, always well-groomed, and to-night,
in the tight-fitting, plum-colored uniform of
the Royal Bengal Bouncers, he completely puts
the home-grown youths in the shade. True,
these inferior lights avenge themselves by telling
the girls that Freddy’s' waist derives its slimness
from the fact that he wears stays —but what of
that ! “ They adds to the simmetery ! ” as the
undertaker said when the two new doctors came
to town. Little Tom strolls off, with one or two
other husbands, to find the refreshment-room.
Supper is over, and things are beginning to liven
up a bit. The band is playing kitchen lancers,
and, to the strains of various music-hall melodies,
the dancers fly round and round, panting, laugh-
ing, and thoroughly roused with excitement —and
champagne. And one sees that in spite of the
gorgeous gowns, the diamonds and the dress
clothes, the old barbaric strain in human nature
still asserts itself. The light in these eyes is the
light that is in Hawaiian eyes when the “Hula
Hula ” begins to grow interesting. The music
breaks off in a final burst, and there is a rush for
the cool air of the garden.
Mrs. Tom and Freddy have found a secluded
seat down by the water’s edge, where there is
plenty of room for two people—if they sit the
right way.
And just below them the tiny waves are lap,
lap, lapping against the rocks, beneath the laugh-
ing moon. . . And whispering couples are coo-
ing and chuckling in every shady nook of the
garden.
And little Tom Scourings and some other hus-
bands are talking horse in the supper-room—and
taking terrible liberties with the sweet champagne.
And the band is playing “Home, Sweet Home’'
—with variations.
Malvern, Yio.
J. H. WAGNER.
